This book is what its author believes to be the only attempt yet made to
put the nglish reader into possession, in clear, compact, and what it
is hoped may prove agreeable, form, of the mythical, legendary, and
poetic traditions of the earliest inhabitants of our islands who have
left us written recordsâ€”the aelic and the ritish elts. t is true
that admirable translations and paraphrases of much of aelic mythical
saga have been recently published, and that Lady harlotte uestâ€s
translation of the Mabinogion has been placed within the reach of the
least wealthy reader. ut these books not merely each cover a portion
only of the whole ground, but, in addition, contain little elucidatory
matter. Their characters stand isolated and unexplained and the details
that would explain them must be sought for with considerable trouble in
the lectures and essays of scholars to learned societies. The reader to
whom this literature is entirely new is introduced, as it were, to
numerous people of whose antecedents he knows nothing and the effect is
often disconcerting enough to make him lay down the volume in despair.

ut here he will at last make the formal acuaintance of all the chief
characters of eltic myth of the aelic gods and the giants against
whom they struggled of the â€hampions of the ed ranchâ€ of lster,
heroes of a martial epopee almost worthy to be placed beside â€the tale
of Troy divineâ€ and of inn and his enians. e will meet also with the
divine and heroic personages of the ancient ritons with their earliest
gods, kin to the members of the aelic antheon as well as with rthur
and his nights, whom he will recognie as no mortal champions, but
belonging to the same mythic company. f all these mighty figures the
histories will be briefly recorded, from the time of their unuestioned
godhood, through their various transformations, to the last doubtful,
dying recognition of them in the present day, as â€fairiesâ€. Thus the
volume will form a kind of handbook to a subect of growing
importanceâ€”the socalled â€eltic enaissanceâ€, which is, after all, no
moreâ€”and, indeed, no lessâ€”than an endeavour to refresh the vitality of
nglish poetry at its most ancient native fount.

The book does not, of course, profess to be for eltic scholars, to
whom, indeed, its author himself owes all that is within it. t aims
only at interesting the reader familiar with the mythologies of reece,
ome, and candinavia in another, and a nearer, source of poetry. ts
authorâ€s wish is to offer those who have fallen, or will fall, under the
attraction of eltic legend and romance, ust such a volume as he
himself would once have welcomed, and for which he sought in vain. t is
his hope that, in choosing from the considerable, though scattered,
translations and commentaries of students of ld aelic and ld elsh,
he has chosen wisely, and that his readers will be able, should they
wish, to use his book as a steppingstone to the authorities themselves.
To that end it is wholly directed and its marginal notes and short
bibliographical appendix follow the same plan. They do not aspire to
anything like completeness, but only to point out the chief sources from
which he himself has drawn.

To acknowledge, as far as possible, such debts is now the authorâ€s
pleasing duty. irst and foremost, he has relied upon the volumes of M.
. dâ€rbois de ubainvilleâ€s ours de Littrature celtiue, and the
ibbert Lectures for  of ohn hys, rofessor of eltic in the
niversity of xford, with their seuel entitled tudies in the
rthurian Legend. rom the writings of Mr. lfred utt he has also
obtained much help. ith regard to direct translations, it seems almost
superfluous to refer to Lady harlotte uestâ€s Mabinogion and Mr. .
. keneâ€s our ncient ooks of ales, or to the work of such
wellknown aelic scholars as Mr. ugene â€urry, r. uno Meyer, r.
hitley tokes, r. rnest indisch, Mr. tandish ayes â€rady to
mention no others, as contained in such publications as the evue
eltiue, the tlantis, and the Transactions of the ssianic
ociety, in Mr. â€radyâ€s ilva adelica, Mr. uttâ€s oyage of
ran, on of ebal, and Miss ullâ€s uchullin aga. ut space is
lacking to do ustice to all. The reader is referred to the marginal
notes and the ppendix for the works of these and other authors, who
will no doubt pardon the use made of their researches to one whose sole
obect has been to gain a larger audience for the studies they have most
at heart.

inally, perhaps, a word should be said upon that vexed uestion, the
transliteration of aelic. s yet there is no universal or consistent
method of spelling. The author has therefore chosen the forms which

t should hardly be necessary to remind the reader of what profound
interest and value to every nation are its earliest legendary and
poetical records. The beautiful myths of reece form a sufficing
example. n threefold manner, they have influenced the destiny of the
people that created them, and of the country of which they were the
imagined theatre. irst, in the ages in which they were still fresh,
belief and pride in them were powerful enough to bring scattered tribes
into confederation. econdly, they gave the inspiration to sculptor and
poet of an art and literature unsurpassed, if not uneualled, by any
other age or race. Lastly, when â€the glory that was reeceâ€ had faded,
and her people had, by dint of successive invasions, perhaps even ceased
to have any right to call themselves ellenes, they have passed over
into the literatures of the modern world, and so given to reece herself
a poetic interest that still makes a petty kingdom of greater account in
the eyes of its compeers than many others far superior to it in extent
and resources.

This permeating influence of the reek poetical mythology, apparent in
all civilied countries, has acted especially upon our own. rom almost
the very dawn of nglish literature, the reek stories of gods and
heroes have formed a large part of the stockintrade of nglish poets.
The inhabitants of lympus occupy, under their betterknown Latin names,
almost as great a space in nglish poetry as they did in that of the
countries to which they were native. rom haucer downwards, they have
captivated the imagination alike of the poets and their hearers. The
magic cauldron of classic myth fed, like the eltic â€railâ€, all who
came to it for sustenance.

t last, however, its potency became somewhat exhausted. lien and
exotic to nglish soil, it degenerated slowly into a convention. n the
shallow hands of the poetasters of the eighteenth century, its figures
became mere puppets. ith every wood a â€groveâ€, and every rustic maid a
â€nymphâ€, one could only expect to find enus armed with patch and
powderpuff, Mars shouldering a musket, and pollo inspiring the
versifierâ€s own trivial strains. The affectation killedâ€”and fortunately
killedâ€”a mode of expression which had become obsolete. mothered by ust
ridicule, and abandoned to the commonplace vocabulary of the inferior
hackwriter, classic myth became a subect which only the greatest poets
could afford to handle.

ut mythology is of such vital need to literature that, deprived of the
store of legend native to southern urope, imaginative writers looked
for a fresh impulse. They turned their eyes to the orth. nspiration
was sought, not from lympus, but from sgard. Moreover, it was believed
that the fount of primeval poetry issuing from candinavian and Teutonic
myth was truly our own, and that we were rightful heirs of it by reason
of the ngloaxon in our blood. nd so, indeed, we are but it is not
our sole heritage. There must also run much elticâ€”that is, truly
ritishâ€”blood in our veins. nd Matthew rnold was probably right in
asserting that, while we owe to the ngloaxon the more practical
ualities that have built up the ritish mpire, we have inherited from
the eltic side that poetic vision which has made nglish literature the
most brilliant since the reek.

e have the right, therefore, to enter upon a new spiritual possession.
nd a splendid one it is The eltic mythology has little of the heavy
crudeness that repels one in Teutonic and candinavian story. t is as
beautiful and graceful as the reek and, unlike the reek, which is the
reflection of a clime and soil which few of us will ever see, it is our
own. ivinities should, surely, seem the inevitable outgrowth of the
land they move in ow strange pollo would appear, naked among
icebergs, or furclad Thor striding under groves of palms ut the
eltic gods and heroes are the natural inhabitants of a ritish
landscape, not seeming foreign and outofplace in a scene where there
is no vine or olive, but â€shading in withâ€ our homely oak and bracken,
gorse and heath.

Thus we gain an altogether fresh interest in the beautiful spots of our
own islands, especially those of the wilder and more mountainous west,
where the older inhabitants of the land lingered longest. axon conuest
obliterated much in astern ritain, and changed more but in the est
of ngland, in ales, in cotland, and especially in legendhaunted
reland, the hills and dales still keep memories of the ancient gods of
the ancient race. ere and there in outh ales and the est of ngland
are regionsâ€”once mysterious and still romanticâ€”which the ritish elts
held to be the homes of gods or outposts of the ther orld. n reland,
not only is there scarcely a place that is not connected in some way
with the traditionary exploits of the â€ed ranch hampionsâ€, or of inn
and his mighty men, but the old deities are still remembered, dwarfed
into fairies, but keeping the same attributes and the same names as of
yore. ordsworthâ€s complaint that, while elion and ssa, lympus and
arnassus are â€in immortal books enrolledâ€, not one nglish mountain,
â€though round our seagirt shore they rise in crowdsâ€, had been â€by the
elestial Muses glorifiedâ€ doubtless seemed true to his own generation.
Thanks to the scholars who have unveiled the ancient aelic and ritish
mythologies, it need not be so for ours. n Ludgate ill, as well as on
many less famous eminences, once stood the temple of the ritish eus. 
mountain not far from ettwsyoed was the ritish lympus, the court
and palace of our ancient gods.

t may well be doubted, however, whether ordsworthâ€s contemporaries
would have welcomed the mythology which was their own by right of birth
as a substitute for that of reece and ome. The inspiration of classic
culture, which ordsworth was one of the first to break with, was still
powerful. ow some of its professors would have held their sides and
roared at the very notion of a ritish mythology Yet, all the time, it
had long been secretly leavening nglish ideas and ideals, none the less
potently because disguised under forms which could be readily
appreciated. opular fancy had rehabilitated the old gods, long banned
by the priestsâ€ bell, book, and candle, under various disguises. They
still lived on in legend as kings of ancient ritain reigning in a
fabulous past anterior to ulius aesarâ€”such were ing Lud, founder of
London ing Lear, whose legend was immortalied by hakespeare ing
rennius, who conuered ome as well as many others who will be found
filling parts in old drama. They still lived on as longdead saints of
the early churches of reland and ritain, whose wonderful attributes
and adventures are, in many cases, only those of their original
namesakes, the old gods, told afresh. nd they still lived on in
another, and a yet more potent, way. Myths of rthur and his cycle of
gods passed into the hands of the orman storytellers, to reappear as
romances of ing rthur and his nights of the Table ound. Thus spread
over civilied urope, their influence was immense. Their primal poetic
impulse is still resonant in our literature we need only instance
Tennyson and winburne as minds that have come under its sway.

This diverse influence of eltic mythology upon nglish poetry and
romance has been elouently set forth by Mr. lton in his rigins of
nglish istory. â€The religion of the ritish tribesâ€, he writes, â€has
exercised an important influence upon literature. The medival romances

humanitiesâ€ and figures of its bright mythology. The elemental powers of
earth and fire, and the spirits which haunted the waves and streams
appear again as kings in the rish nnals, or as saints and hermits in

ir edivere, betray their mighty origin by the attributes they retained
as heroes of romance. t was a goddess, â€ea uaedam phantasticaâ€, who
bore the wounded rthur to the peaceful valley. â€There was little
sunlight on its woods and streams, and the nights were dark and gloomy
for want of the moon and stars.â€ This is the country of beron and of
ir uon of ordeaux. t is the dreamy forest of rden. n an older
mythology, it was the realm of a ing of hadows, the country of wyn ap
udd, who rode as ir uyon in the â€airie ueeneâ€â€”

           â€nd knighthood took of good ir uonâ€s hand,
            hen with ing beron he came to airylandâ€.â€

To trace elsh and rish kings and saints and hermits back to â€the
elemental powers of earth and fire, and the spirits that haunted the
woods and streamsâ€ of eltic imagination, and to disclose primitive
pagan deities under the medival and hristian trappings of â€ing
rthurâ€s nightsâ€ will necessarily fall within the scope of this volume.
ut meanwhile the reader will probably be asking what evidence there is
that apocryphal ritish kings like Lear and Lud, and uestionable rish

The answer to his uestion will be found in the next chapter.

e may begin by asserting with confidence that Mr. lton has touched
upon a part only of the material on which we may draw, to reconstruct
the ancient ritish mythology. Luckily, we are not wholly dependent upon
the difficult tasks of resolving the fabled deeds of apocryphal rish
and ritish kings who reigned earlier than t. atrick or before ulius
aesar into their original form of eltic myths, of sifting the
attributes and miracles of doubtfully historical saints, or of
separating the primitive pagan elements in the legends of rthur and his
nights from the embellishments added by the romancewriters. e have,
in addition to theseâ€”which we may for the present put upon one side as
secondaryâ€”sources, a mass of genuine early writings which, though
posthristian in the form in which they now exist, none the less
descend from the preceding pagan age. These are contained in vellum and
parchment manuscripts long preserved from destruction in mansions and
monasteries in reland, cotland, and ales, and only during the last
century brought to light, copied, and translated by the patient labours
of scholars who have grappled with the longobsolete dialects in which
they were transcribed.

Many of these volumes are curious miscellanies. sually the one book of
a great house or monastic community, everything was copied into it that
the scholar of the family or brotherhood thought to be best worth
preserving. ence they contain matter of the most diverse kind. There
are translations of portions of the ible and of the classics, and of
such then popular books as eoffrey of Monmouthâ€s and enniusâ€ istories
of ritain lives of famous saints, together with works attributed to
them poems and romances of which, under a thin disguise, the old aelic
and ritish gods are the heroes together with treatises on all the
subects then studiedâ€”grammar, prosody, law, history, geography,
chronology, and the genealogies of important chiefs.

The maority of these documents were put together during a period which,
roughly speaking, lasted from the beginning of the twelfth century to
the end of the sixteenth. n reland, in ales, and, apparently, also in
cotland, it was a time of literary revival after the turmoils of the
previous epoch. n reland, the orsemen, after long ravaging, had
settled peacefully down, while in ales, the orman onuest had
rendered the country for the first time comparatively uiet. The
scattered remains of history, lay and ecclesiastical, of science, and of
legend were gathered together.

f the rish manuscripts, the earliest, and, for our purposes, the most
important, on account of the great store of ancient aelic mythology
which, in spite of its dilapidated condition, it still contains, is in
the possession of the oyal rish cademy. nluckily, it is reduced to a
fragment of one hundred and thirtyeight pages, but this remnant
preserves a large number of romances relating to the old gods and heroes
of reland. mong other things, it contains a complete account of the
epical saga called the Tin  huailgn, the â€aiding of the attle
of ooleyâ€, in which the hero, uchulainn, performed his greatest feats.
This manuscript is called the ook of the un ow, from the tradition
that it was copied from an earlier book written upon the skin of a
favourite animal belonging to aint iaran, who lived in the seventh
century. n entry upon one of its pages reveals the name of its scribe,
one Maelmuiri, whom we know to have been killed by robbers in the church
of lonmacnois in the year .

ar more voluminous, and but little less ancient, is the ook of
Leinster, said to have been compiled in the early part of the twelfth
century by inn mac orman, ishop of ildare. This also contains an
account of uchulainnâ€s mighty deeds which supplements the older version
in the ook of the un ow. f somewhat less importance from the point
of view of the student of aelic mythology come the ook of allymote
and the Yellow ook of Lecan, belonging to the end of the fourteenth
century, and the ooks of Lecan and of Lismore, both attributed to the
fifteenth. esides these six great collections, there survive many other
manuscripts which also contain ancient mythical lore. n one of these,
dating from the fifteenth century, is to be found the story of the
attle of Moytura, fought between the gods of reland and their enemies,
the omors, or demons of the deep sea.

The cottish manuscripts, preserved in the dvocatesâ€ Library at
dinburgh, date back in some cases as far as the fourteenth century,
though the maority of them belong to the fifteenth and sixteenth. They
corroborate the rish documents, add to the uchulainn saga, and make a
more special subect of the other heroic cycle, that which relates the
not less wonderful deeds of inn, ssian, and the enians. They also
contain stories of other characters, who, more ancient than either inn
or uchulainn, are the Tuatha  anann, the godtribe of the ancient
aels.

The elsh documents cover about the same period as the rish and the
cottish. our of these stand out from the rest, as most important. The
oldest is the lack ook of aermarthen, which dates from the third
uarter of the twelfth century the ook of neurin, which was written
late in the thirteenth the ook of Taliesin, assigned to the
fourteenth and the ed ook of ergest, compiled by various persons
during that century and the one following it. The first three of these
â€our ncient ooks of alesâ€ are small in sie, and contain poems
attributed to the great traditional bards of the sixth century, Myrddin,
Taliesin, and neurin. The lastâ€”the ed ook of ergestâ€”is far larger.
n it are to be found elsh translations of the ritish hronicles the
oftmentioned Triads, verses celebrating famous traditionary persons or
things ancient poems attributed to Llywarch n and, of priceless
value to any study of our subect, the socalled Mabinogion, stories in
which large portions of the old ritish mythology are worked up into
romantic form.

The whole bulk, therefore, of the native literature bearing upon the
mythology of the ritish slands may be attributed to a period which
lasted from the beginning of the twelfth century to the end of the
sixteenth. ut even the commencement of this era will no doubt seem far
too late a day to allow authenticity to matter which ought to have
vastly preceded it. The date, however, merely marks the final redaction
of the contents of the manuscripts into the form in which they now
exist, without bearing at all upon the time of their authorship.
vowedly copies of ancient poems and tales from much older manuscripts,
the present books no more fix the period of the original composition of
their contents than the presence of a portion of the anterbury Tales
in a modern anthology of nglish poetry would assign haucer to the
present year of grace.

This may be proved both directly and inferentially. n some
instancesâ€”as in that of an elegy upon aint olumba in the ook of the
un owâ€”the dates of authorship are actually given. n others, we may
depend upon evidence which, if not uite so absolute, is nearly as
convincing. ven where the writer does not state that he is copying from
older manuscripts, it is obvious that this must have been the case, from
the glosses in his version. The scribes of the earlier aelic
manuscripts very often found, in the documents from which they
themselves were copying, words so archaic as to be unintelligible to the
readers of their own period. To render them comprehensible, they were
obliged to insert marginal notes which explained these obsolete words by
reference to other manuscripts more ancient still. ften the medival
copyists have ignorantly moved these notes from the margin into the
text, where they remain, like philological fossils, to give evidence of
previous forms of life. The documents from which they were taken have
perished, leaving the medival copies as their sole record. n the elsh
Mabinogion the same process is apparent. eculiarities in the existing
manuscripts show plainly enough that they must have been copied from
some more archaic text. esides this, they are, as they at present
stand, obviously made up of earlier tales pieced together. lmost as
clearly as the aelic manuscripts, the elsh point us back to older and
more primitive forms.

The ancient legends of the ael and the riton are thus shown to have
been no mere inventions of scholarly monks in the Middle ges. e have
now to trace, if possible, the date, not necessarily of their first
appearance on menâ€s lips, but of their first redaction into writing in
approximately the form in which we have them now.

ircumstantial evidence can be adduced to prove that the most important
portions both of aelic and ritish early literature can be safely
relegated to a period of several centuries prior to their nowexisting
record. ur earliest version of the episode of the Tin  huailgn,
which is the nucleus and centre of the ancient aelic heroic cycle of
which uchulainn, fortissimus heros cotorum, is the principal figure,
is found in the twelfthcentury ook of the un ow. ut legend tells us
that at the beginning of the seventh century the aga had not only been
composed, but had actually become so obsolete as to have been forgotten
by the bards. Their leader, one enchan Torpeist, a historical
character, and chief bard of reland at that time, obtained permission
from the aints to call ergus, uchulainnâ€s contemporary, and a chief
actor in the â€aidâ€, from the dead, and received from the resurrected
hero a true and full version. This tradition, dealing with a real
personage, surely shows that the story of the Tin was known before
the time of enchan, and probably preserves the fact, either that his
version of uchulainnâ€s famous deeds became the accepted one, or that he
was the first to reduce it to writing. n eually suggestive
consideration approximately fixes for us the earliest redaction of the
elsh mythological prose tales called the â€Mabinogionâ€, or, more
correctly speaking, the â€our ranches of the Mabinogiâ€. n none of
these is there the slightest mention, or apparently the least knowledge,
of rthur, around whom and whose supposed contemporaries centres the
mass of ritish legend as it was transmitted by the elsh to the
ormans. These mysterious mythological records must in all probability,
therefore, antedate the rthurian cycle of myth, which was already being
put into form in the sixth century. n the other hand, the characters of
the â€our ranchesâ€ are mentioned without commentâ€”as though they were
personages with whom no one could fail to be familiarâ€”in the supposed
sixthcentury poems contained in those â€our ncient ooks of alesâ€ in
which are found the first meagre references to the ritish hero.

uch considerations as these throw back, with reasonable certainty, the
existence of the rish and elsh poems and prose tales, in something
like their present shape, to a period antedating the seventh century.

ut this, again, means only that the myths, traditions, and legends were
current at that to us early, but to them, in their actual substance,
late date, in literary form.  mythology must always be far older than
the oldest verses and stories that celebrate it. laborate poems and
sagas are not made in a day, or in a year. The legends of the aelic and
ritish gods and heroes could not have sprung, like thena from the head
of eus, fullborn out of some poetâ€s brain. The bard who first put them
into artistic shape was setting down the primitive traditions of his
race. e may therefore venture to describe them as not of the twelfth
century or of the seventh, but as of a prehistoric and immemorial
antiuity.

nternal evidence bears this out. n examination of both the aelic and
ritish legendary romances shows, under embellishing details added by
later hands, an inner core of primeval thought which brings them into
line with the similar ideas of other races in the earliest stage of
culture. Their â€local colourâ€ may be that of their last â€editorâ€, but
their â€plotsâ€ are premedival, prehristian, prehistoric. The
characters of early aelic legend belong to the same stamp of
imagination that created lympian and Titan, sir and tun. e must go
far to the back of civilied thought to find parallels to such a story
as that in which the ritish sungod, struck by a rival in love with a
poisoned spear, is turned into an eagle, from whose wound great pieces
of carrion are continually failing.

This aspect of the eltic literary records was clearly seen, and
elouently expressed, by Matthew rnold in his tudy of eltic
Literature. e was referring to the elsh side, but his image holds
good eually for the aelic. â€The first thing that strikes oneâ€, he
says, â€in reading the Mabinogion is how evidently the medival
storyteller is pillaging an antiuity of which he does not fully
possess the secret he is like a peasant building his hut on the site of
alicarnassus or phesus he builds, but what he builds is full of
materials of which he knows not the history, or knows by a glimmering
tradition merely stones â€not of this buildingâ€, but of an older
architecture, greater, cunninger, more maestical.â€ is heroes â€are no
medival personages they belong to an older, pagan, mythological
worldâ€. o, too, with the figures, however euhemeried, of the three
great aelic cycles that of the Tuatha  anann, of the eroes of
lster, of inn and the enians. Their divinity outshines their
humanity through their masks may be seen the faces of gods.

Yet, gods as they are, they had taken on the semblance of mortality by
the time their histories were fixed in the form in which we have them
now. Their earliest records, if those could be restored to us, would
doubtless show them eternal and undying, changing their shapes at will,
but not passing away. ut the posthristian copyists, whether rish or
elsh, would not countenance this. ence we have the singular paradox of
the deaths of mmortals. There is hardly one of the figures of either
the aelic or the ritish antheon whose demise is not somewhere
recorded. sually they fell in the unceasing battles between the
divinities of darkness and of light. Their deaths in earlier cycles of
myth, however, do not preclude their appearance in later ones. nly,
indeed, with the closing of the lips of the last mortal who preserved
his tradition can the life of a god be truly said to end.

ut, before proceeding to recount the myths of the â€ncient ritonsâ€, it
will be well to decide what people, exactly, we mean by that loose but
convenient phrase. e have, all of us, vague ideas of ncient ritons,
recollected, doubtless, from our schoolbooks. There we saw their
pictures as, painted with woad, they paddled coracles, or drove scythed
chariots through legions of astonished omans. Their ruids,
whitebearded and wearing long, white robes, cut the mistletoe with a
golden sickle at the time of the full moon, or, less innocently
employed, made bonfires of human beings shut up in gigantic figures of
wickerwork.

uch picturesue details were little short of the sumtotal, not only of
our own knowledge of the subect, but also of that of our teachers.
ractically all their information concerning the ancient inhabitants of
ritain was taken from the ommentaries of ulius aesar. o far as it
went, it was no doubt correct but it did not go far. aesarâ€s interest
in our ritish ancestors was that of a general who was his own
warcorrespondent rather than that of an exhaustive and painstaking
scientist. t has been reserved for modern archologists, philologists,
and ethnologists to give us a fuller account of the ncient ritons.

The inhabitants of our islands previous to the oman invasion are
generally described as â€eltsâ€. ut they must have been largely a mixed
race and the people with whom they mingled must have modified to
someâ€”and perhaps to a largeâ€”extent their physiue, their customs, and
their language.

peculation has run somewhat wild over the uestion of the composition
of the arly ritons. ut out of the clash of rival theories there
emerges oneâ€”and one onlyâ€”which may be considered as scientifically
established. e have certain proof of two distinct human stocks in the
ritish slands at the time of the oman onuest and so great an
authority as rofessor uxley has given his opinion that there is no
evidence of any others.

The earliest of these two races would seem to have inhabited our islands
from the most ancient times, and may, for our purpose, be described as
aboriginal. t was the people that built the â€long barrowsâ€ and which
is variously called by ethnologists the berian, Mediterranean, erber,
asue, ilurian, or uskarian race. n physiue it was short, swarthy,
darkhaired, darkeyed, and longskulled its language belonged to the
class called â€amiticâ€, the surviving types of which are found among the
allas, byssinians, erbers, and other orth frican tribes and it
seems to have come originally from some part either of astern,
orthern, or entral frica. preading thence, it was probably the first
people to inhabit the alley of the ile, and it sent offshoots into
yria and sia Minor. The earliest ellenes found it in reece under the
name of â€elasgoiâ€ the earliest Latins in taly, as the â€truscansâ€
and the ebrews in alestine, as the â€ittitesâ€. t spread northward
through urope as far as the altic, and westward, along the tlas
chain, to pain, rance, and our own islands. n many countries it
reached a comparatively high level of civiliation, but in ritain its
development must have been early checked. e can discern it as an
agricultural rather than a pastoral people, still in the tone ge,
dwelling in totemistic tribes on hills whose summits it fortified
elaborately, and whose slopes it cultivated on what is called the
â€terrace systemâ€, and having a primitive culture which ethnologists
think to have much resembled that of the present hilltribes of outhern
ndia. t held our islands till the coming of the elts, who fought
with the aborigines, dispossessed them of the more fertile parts,
subugated them, even amalgamated with them, but certainly never
extirpated them. n the time of the omans they were still practically
independent in outh ales. n reland they were long unconuered, and
are found as allies rather than serfs of the aels, ruling their own
provinces, and preserving their own customs and religion. or, in spite
of all the successive invasions of reat ritain and reland, are they
yet extinct, or so merged as to have lost their type, which is still the
predominant one in many parts of the west both of ritain and reland,
and is believed by some ethnologists to be generally upon the increase
all over ngland.

The second of the two races was the exact opposite to the first. t was
the tall, fair, lighthaired, blue or grayeyed, broadheaded people
called, popularly, the â€eltsâ€, who belonged in speech to the â€ryanâ€
family, their language finding its affinities in Latin, reek, Teutonic,
lavic, the end of ncient ersia, and the anscrit of ncient ndia.
ts original home was probably somewhere in entral urope, along the
course of the upper anube, or in the region of the lps. The â€round
barrowsâ€ in which it buried its dead, or deposited their burnt ashes,
differ in shape from the â€long barrowsâ€ of the earlier race. t was in a
higher stage of culture than the â€beriansâ€, and introduced into ritain
brone and silver, and, perhaps, some of the more lately domesticated
animals.

oth berians and elts were divided into numerous tribes, but there is
nothing to show that there was any great diversity among the former. t
is otherwise with the elts, who were separated into two main branches
which came over at different times. The earliest were the oidels, or
aels the second, the rythons, or ritons. etween these two branches
there was not only a dialectical, but probably, also, a considerable
physical difference. ome anthropologists even postulate a different
shape of skull. ithout necessarily admitting this, there is reason to
suppose a difference of build and of colour of hair. ith regard to
this, we have the evidence of Latin writersâ€”of Tacitus, who tells us
that the â€aledoniansâ€ of the orth differed from the outhern ritons
in being largerlimbed and redderhaired, and of trabo, who
described the tribes in the interior of ritain as taller than the

loosely knit. ually do the classic authorities agree in recogniing
the â€iluresâ€ of outh ales as an entirely different race from any
other in ritain. The dark complexions and curly hair of these berians
seemed to Tacitus to prove them immigrants from pain.

rofessor hys also puts forward evidence to show that the oidels and
the rythons had already separated before they first left aul for our
islands. e finds them as two distinct peoples there. e do not
expect so much nowadays from â€the merest schoolboyâ€ as we did in
Macaulayâ€s time, but even the modern descendant of that paragon could
probably tell us that all aul was divided into three parts, one of
which was inhabited by the elgae, another by the uitani, and the
third by those who called themselves eltae, but were termed alli by
the omans and that they all differed from one another in language,
customs, and laws. f these, rofessor hys identifies the elgae
with the rythons, and the eltae with the oidels, the third people,
the uitani, being noneltic and nonryan, part of the great
amiticspeaking berian stock. The eltae, with their oidelic
dialect of eltic, which survives today in the aelic languages of
reland, cotland, and the sle of Man, were the first to come over to
ritain, pushed forward, probably, by the elgae, who, aesar tells us,
were the bravest of the auls. ere they conuered the native
berians, driving them out of the fertile parts into the rugged
districts of the north and west. Later came the elgae themselves,
compelled by press of population and they, bringing better weapons and
a higher civiliation, treated the oidels as those had treated the
berians. Thus harried, the oidels probably combined with the berians
against what was now the common foe, and became to a large degree
amalgamated with them. The result was that during the oman domination
the ritish slands were roughly divided with regard to race as follows
The rythons, or second eltic race, held all ritain south of the
Tweed, with the exception of the extreme west, while the first eltic
race, the oidelic, had most of reland, as well as the sle of Man,
umberland, the est ighlands, ornwall, evon, and orth ales. orth
of the rampians lived the icts, who were probably more or less
oidelicied berians, the aboriginal race also holding out, unmixed, in
outh ales and parts of reland.

t is now time to decide what, for the purposes of this book, it will be
best to call the two different branches of the elts, and their
languages. ith such familiar terms as â€aelâ€ and â€ritonâ€, â€aelicâ€ and
â€ritishâ€, ready to our hands, it seems pedantic to insist upon the more
technical â€oidelâ€ and â€rythonâ€, â€oidelicâ€ and â€rythonicâ€. The
difficulty is that the words â€aelâ€ and â€aelicâ€ have been so long
popularly used to designate only the modern â€oidelsâ€ of cotland and
their language, that they may create confusion when also applied to the
people and languages of reland and the sle of Man. imilarly, the
words â€ritonâ€ and â€ritishâ€ have come to mean, at the present day, the
people of the whole of the ritish slands, though they at first only
signified the inhabitants of ngland, entral ales, the Lowlands of
cotland, and the rythonic colony in rittany. owever, the words
â€oidelâ€ and â€rythonâ€, with their derivatives, are so clumsy that it
will probably prove best to use the neater terms. n this volume,
therefore, the â€oidelsâ€ of reland, cotland, and the sle of Man are
our â€aelsâ€ and the â€rythonsâ€ of ngland and ales are our â€ritonsâ€.

e get the earliest accounts of the life of the inhabitants of the
ritish slands from two sources. The first is a foreign one, that of
the Latin writers. ut the omans only really knew the outhern ritons,
whom they describe as similar in physiue and customs to the ontinental
auls, with whom, indeed, they considered them to be identical. t
the time they wrote, colonies of elgae were still settling upon the
coasts of ritain opposite to aul. oman information grew scantier
as it approached the all, and of the orthern tribes they seem to have
had only such knowledge as they gathered through occasional warfare with
them. They describe them as entirely barbarous, naked and tattooed,
living by the chase alone, without towns, houses, or fields, without
government or family life, and regarding iron as an ornament of value,
as other, more civilied peoples regarded gold. s for reland, it
never came under their direct observation, and we are entirely dependent
upon its native writers for information as to the manners and customs of
the aels. t may be considered convincing proof of the authenticity of
the descriptions of life contained in the ancient aelic manuscripts
that they corroborate so completely the observations of the Latin
writers upon the ritons and auls. eading the two side by side, we may
largely reconstruct the common civiliation of the elts.

oughly speaking, one may compare it with the civiliation of the
reeks, as described by omer. oth peoples were in the tribal and
pastoral stage of culture, in which the chiefs are the great
cattleowners round whom their less wealthy fellows gather. oth wear
much the same attire, use the same kind of weapons, and fight in the
same mannerâ€”from the warchariot, a vehicle already obsolete even in
reland by the first century of the hristian era. attles are fought
singlehanded between chiefs, the illarmed common people contributing
little to their result, and less to their history. uch chiefs are said
to be divinely descendedâ€”sons, even, of the immortal gods. Their
tremendous feats are sung by the bards, who, like the omeric poets,
were privileged persons, inferior only to the warlord. ncient reek
and ncient elt had very much the same conceptions of life, both as
regards this world and the next.

e may gather much detailed information of the early inhabitants of the
ritish slands from our various authorities. Their clothes, which
consisted, according to the Latin writers, of a blouse with sleeves,
trousers fitting closely round the ankles, and a shawl or cloak,
fastened at the shoulder with a brooch, were made either of thick felt
or of woven cloth dyed with various brilliant colours. The writer
iodorus tells us that they were crossed with little suares and lines,
â€as though they had been sprinkled with flowersâ€. They were, in fact,
like â€tartansâ€, and we may believe arro, who tells us that they â€made a
gaudy showâ€. The men alone seem to have worn hats, which were of soft
felt, the womenâ€s hair being uncovered, and tied in a knot behind. n
time of battle, the men also dispensed with any headcovering, brushing
their abundant hair forward into a thick mass, and dyeing it red with a
soap made of goatâ€s fat and beech ashes, until they looked says
iceroâ€s tutor osidonius, who visited ritain about  .. less like
human beings than wild men of the woods. oth sexes were fond of
ornaments, which took the form of gold bracelets, rings, pins, and
brooches, and of beads of amber, glass, and et. Their knives, daggers,
spearheads, axes, and swords were made of brone or iron their shields
were the same round target used by the ighlanders at the battle of
ulloden and they seem also to have had a kind of lasso to which a
hammershaped ball was attached, and which they used as the auchos of
outh merica use their bola. Their warchariots were made of wicker,
the wooden wheels being armed with sickles of brone. These were drawn
either by two or four horses, and were large enough to hold several
persons in each. tanding in these, they rushed along the enemyâ€s lines,
hurling darts, and driving the scythes against all who came within
reach. The omans were much impressed by the skill of the drivers, who
â€could check their horses at full speed on a steep incline, and turn
them in an instant, and could run along the pole, and stand on the yoke,
and then get back into their chariots again without a momentâ€s
delayâ€.

ith these accounts of the oman writers we may compare the picture of
the aelic hero, uchulainn, as the ancient rish writers describe him
dressed and armed for battle. lorified by the bard, he yet wears
essentially the same costume and euipment which the classic historians
and geographers described more soberly. â€is gorgeous raiment that he
wore in great conventionsâ€ consisted of â€a fair crimson tunic of five
plies and fringed, with a long pin of white silver, goldenchased and
patterned, shining as if it had been a luminous torch which for its
blaing property and brilliance men might not endure to see. ext his
skin, a bodyvest of silk, bordered and fringed all round with gold,
with silver, and with white brone, which vest came as far as the upper
edge of his russetcoloured kilt.... bout his neck were a hundred
linklets of red gold that flashed again, with pendants hanging from
them. is headgear was adorned with a hundred mixed carbuncle ewels,
strung.â€ e carried â€a trusty special shield, in hue dark crimson, and
in its circumference armed with a pure white silver rim. t his left
side a long and goldenhilted sword. eside him, in the chariot, a
lengthy spear together with a keen, aggressionboding avelin, fitted
with hurling thong, with rivets of white brone.â€ nother passage of
aelic saga describes his chariot. t was made of fine wood, with
wickerwork, moving on wheels of white brone. t had a high rounded
frame of creaking copper, a strong curved yoke of gold, and a pole of
white silver, with mountings of white brone. The yellow reins were
plaited, and the shafts were as hard and straight as swordblades.

n like manner the ancient rish writers have made glorious the halls
and fortresses of their mythical kings. Like the palaces of riam, of
Menelaus, and of dysseus, they gleam with gold and gems. onchobar,
the legendary ing of lster in its golden age, had three such â€housesâ€
at main Macha. f the one called the â€ed ranchâ€, we are told that it
contained nine compartments of red yew, partitioned by walls of brone,
all grouped around the kingâ€s private chamber, which had a ceiling of
silver, and brone pillars adorned with gold and carbuncles. ut the
far less magnificent accounts of the Latin writers have, no doubt, more
truth in them than such lavish pictures. They described the ritons they
knew as living in villages of beehive huts, roofed with fern or thatch,
from which, at the approach of an enemy, they retired to the local
dn. This, so far from being elaborate, merely consisted of a round or
oval space fenced in with palisades and earthworks, and situated either
upon the top of a hill or in the midst of a not easily traversable
morass. e may see the remains of such strongholds in many parts of
nglandâ€”notable ones are the â€castlesâ€ of mesbury, vebury, and ld
arum in iltshire, aint atherineâ€s ill, near inchester, and aint
eorgeâ€s ill, in urreyâ€”and it is probable that, in spite of the eltic
praisers of past days, the â€palacesâ€ of main Macha and of Tara were
very like them.

The eltic customs were, like the omeric, those of the primitive world.
ll land though it may have theoretically belonged to the chief was
cultivated in common. This community of possessions is stated by
aesar to have extended to their wives but the imputation cannot be
said to have been proved. n the contrary, in the stories of both
branches of the eltic race, women seem to have taken a higher place in
menâ€s estimation, and to have enoyed far more personal liberty, than
among the omeric reeks. The idea may have arisen from a
misunderstanding of some of the curious eltic customs. escent seems to
have been traced through the maternal rather than through the paternal
line, a very unryan procedure which some believe to have been borrowed
from another race. The parental relation was still further lessened by
the custom of sending children to be brought up outside the family in
which they were born, so that they had fosterparents to whom they were
as much, or even more, attached than to their natural ones.

Their political state, mirroring their family life, was not less
primitive. There was no central tribunal. isputes were settled within
the families in which they occurred, while, in the case of graver
inuries, the inured party or his nearest relation could kill the
culprit or exact a fine from him. s families increased in number, they
became petty tribes, often at war with one another.  defeated tribe had
to recognie the sovereignty of the head man of the conuering tribe,
and a succession of such victories exalted him into the position of a
chief of his district. ut even then, though his decision was the whole
of the law, he was little more than the mouthpiece of public opinion.

The ancient inhabitants of ritainâ€”the aelic and ritish eltsâ€”have
been already described as forming a branch of what are roughly called
the â€ryansâ€. This name has, however, little reference to race, and
really signifies the speakers of a group of languages which can be all
shown to be connected, and to descend remotely from a single sourceâ€”a
hypothetical mothertongue spoken by a hypothetical people which we term
â€ryanâ€, or, more correctly, â€ndouropeanâ€. This primeval speech,
evolved, probably, upon some part of the great plain which stretches
from the mountains of entral urope to the mountains of entral sia,
has spread, superseding, or amalgamating with the tongues of other
races, until branches of it are spoken over almost the whole of urope
and a great portion of sia. ll the various Latin, reek, lavic,
Teutonic, and eltic languages are â€ryanâ€, as well as ersian and other
siatic dialects derived from the ancient â€endâ€, and the numerous
ndian languages which trace their origin to anscrit.

ot very long ago, it was supposed that this common descent of language
involved a common descent of blood.  real brotherhood was
enthusiastically claimed for all the principal uropean nations, who
were also invited to recognie indus and ersians as their longlost
cousins. ince then, it has been conceded that, while the ryan speech
survived, though greatly modified, the ryan blood might well have
disappeared, diluted beyond recognition by crossing with the other races
whom the ryans conuered, or among whom they more or less peacefully
settled. s a matter of fact, there are no uropean nationsâ€”perhaps no
people at all except a few remote savage tribesâ€”which are not made up of
the most diverse elements. ryan and nonryan long ago blended
inextricably, to form by their fusion new peoples.

ut, ust as the ryan speech influenced the new languages, and the
ryan customs the new civiliations, so we can still discern in the
religions of the ryanspeaking nations similar ideas and expressions
pointing to an original source of mythological conceptions. ence,
whether we investigate the mythology of the indus, the reeks, the
Teutons, or the elts, we find the same mythological groundwork. n
each, we see the powers of nature personified, and endowed with human
form and attributes, though bearing, with few exceptions, different
names. Like the edic brahmans, the reek and Latin poets, and the orse
scalds, the eltic bardsâ€”whether aels or ritonsâ€”imagined the sky, the
sun, the moon, the earth, the sea, and the dark underworld, as well as
the mountains, the streams and the woods, to be ruled by beings like
their own chiefs, but infinitely more powerful every passion, as ar
and Love, and every art, as oetry and mithcraft, had its divine
founder, teacher, and exponent and of all these deities and their
imagined children, they wove the poetical and allegorical romances which
form the subect of the present volume.

Like other nations, too, whether ryan or nonryan, the elts had,
besides their mythology, a religion. t is not enough to tell tales of
shadowy gods they must be made visible by sculpture, housed in groves
or temples, served with ritual, and propitiated with sacrifices, if one
is to hope for their favours. very cult must have its priests living by
the altar.

The priests of the elts are wellknown to us by name as the â€ruidsâ€â€”a
word derived from a root  which signifies a tree, and especially the
oak, in several ryan languages. This is generallyâ€”though not by all
scholarsâ€”taken as proving that they paid an especial veneration to the
king of trees. t is true that the mistletoeâ€”that strange parasite upon
the oakâ€”was prominent among their â€herbs of powerâ€, and played a part in
their ritual but this is eually true of other ryan nations. y
the orse it was held sacred to the god alder, while the omans
believed it to be the â€golden boughâ€ that gave access to ades.

The accounts both of the Latin and aelic writers give us a fairly
complete idea of the nature of the ruids, and especially of the high
estimation in which they were held. They were at once the priests, the
physicians, the wiards, the diviners, the theologians, the scientists,
and the historians of their tribes. ll spiritual power and all human
knowledge were vested in them, and they ranked second only to the kings
and chiefs. They were freed from all contribution to the tate, whether
by tribute or service in war, so that they might the better apply
themselves to their divine offices. Their decisions were absolutely
final, and those who disobeyed them were laid under a terrible
excommunication or â€boycottâ€. lassic writers tell us how they
lorded it in aul, where, no doubt, they borrowed splendour by imitating
their more civilied neighbours. Men of the highest rank were proud to
cast aside the insignia of mere mortal honour to oin the company of
those who claimed to be the direct mediators with the skygod and the
thundergod, and who must have resembled the ecclesiastics of medival
urope in the days of their greatest power, combining, like them,
spiritual and temporal dignities, and possessing the highest culture of
their age. Yet it was not among these ruids of aul, with their
splendid temples and vestments and their elaborate rituals, that the
metropolis of ruidism was to be sought. e learn from aesar that the
allic ruids believed their religion to have come to them, originally,
from ritain, and that it was their practice to send their â€theological
studentsâ€ across the hannel to learn its doctrines at their purest
source. To trace a cult backwards is often to take a retrograde
course in culture, and it was no doubt in ritainâ€”which liny the lder
tells us â€might have taught magic to ersiaâ€â€”that the sufficiently
primitive and savage rites of the ruids of aul were preserved in their
still more savage and primitive forms. t is curious corroboration of
this alleged ritish origin of ruidism that the ancient rish also
believed their ruidism to have come from the sister island. Their
heroes and seers are described as only gaining the highest knowledge by
travelling to lba. owever this may be, we may take it as certain
that this ruidism was the accepted religion of the eltic race.

ertain scholars look deeper for its origin, holding its dark
superstitions and savage rites to bear the stamp of lower minds than
those of the poetic and manly elts. rofessor hys inclines to see
three forms of religion in the ritish slands at the time of the oman

f the famous teaching of the ruids we know little, owing to their

The same great gods were, no doubt, adored by all the elts, not only of

f the two eltic races that settled in our islands, it is the earlier,
the aels, that has best preserved its old mythology. t is true that we
have in few cases such detailed account of the aelic gods as we gain of
the ellenic deities from the reek poets, of the ndian evas from the
ig eda, or of the orse sir from the ddas. Yet none the less may we
draw from the ancient rish manuscripts uite enough information to
enable us to set forth their figures with some clearness. e find them,
as might have been anticipated, very much like the divine hierarchies of
other ryan peoples.

e also find them separated into two opposing camps, a division common
to all the ryan religions. ust as the lympians struggled with the
iants, the sir fought the tuns, and the evas the suras, so there
is warfare in the aelic spiritual world between two superhuman hosts.
n one side are ranged the gods of day, light, life, fertility, wisdom,
and good on the other, the demons of night, darkness, death,
barrenness, and evil. The first were the great spirits symboliing the
beneficial aspects of nature and the arts and intelligence of man the
second were the hostile powers thought to be behind such baneful
manifestations as storm and fog, drought and disease. The first are
ranged as a divine family round a goddess called anu, from whom they
took their wellknown name of Tuatha  anann, â€Tribeâ€ or â€olk
of the oddess anuâ€. The second owned allegiance to a female divinity
called omnu their king, ndech, is described as her son, and they are
all called â€omnuâ€s godsâ€. The word â€omnuâ€ appears to have signified
the abyss or the deep sea, and the same idea is also expressed in
their betterknown name of â€omorsâ€, derived from two aelic words
meaning â€under seaâ€. The waste of water seems to have always
impressed the elts with the sense of primeval ancientness it was
connected in their minds with vastness, darkness, and monstrous
birthsâ€”the very antithesis of all that was symbolied by the earth, the
sky, and the sun.

Therefore the omors were held to be more ancient than the gods, before
whom they were, however, destined to fall in the end. ffspring of
â€haos and ld ightâ€, they were, for the most part, huge and deformed.
ome had but one arm and one leg apiece, while others had the heads of
goats, horses, or bulls. The most famous, and perhaps the most
terrible of them all was alor, whose father is said to have been one
uarainech, that is, the â€cowfacedâ€, and who combined in himself
the two classical rles of the yclops and the Medusa. Though he had two
eyes, one was always kept shut, for it was so venomous that it slew
anyone on whom its look fell. This malignant uality of alorâ€s eye was
not natural to him, but was the result of an accident. rged by
curiosity, he once looked in at the window of a house where his fatherâ€s
sorcerers were preparing a magic potion, and the poisonous smoke from
the cauldron reached his eye, infecting it with so much of its own
deadly nature as to make it disastrous to others. either god nor giant
seems to have been exempt from its dangers so that alor was only
allowed to live on condition that he kept his terrible eye shut. n days
of battle he was placed opposite to the enemy, the lid of the destroying
eye was lifted up with a hook, and its gae withered all who stood

This rule, however, as to the hideousness of the omors had its

The people of the goddess anu were not the first divine inhabitants of
reland. thers had been before them, dwellers in â€the dark backward and
abysm of timeâ€. n this the eltic mythology resembles those of other
nations, in almost all of which we find an old, dim realm of gods
standing behind the reigning antheon. uch were ronos and the Titans,
dispossessed by the eus who seemed, even to esiod, something of a

anterior to the Tuatha  anann. The first of these was called â€The

according to eltic ideasâ€”from the ther orld, and landed in reland

grew from one plain to four, and acuired seven new lakes which was
fortunate, for the race of artholon increased from fortyeight members
to five thousand, in spite of battles with the omors.

These would seem to have been inevitable. hatever gods ruled, they
found themselves in eternal opposition to the notgodsâ€”the powers of
darkness, winter, evil, and death. The race of artholon warred against
them with success. t the lain of th, artholon defeated their leader,
a gigantic demon called ichol the ootless, and dispersed his deformed
and monstrous host. fter this there was uiet for three hundred years.
Thenâ€”upon the same fatal first of Mayâ€”there began a mysterious epidemic,
which lasted a week, and destroyed them all. n premonition of their

the more easily bury those that died. Their funeralplace is still

formerly known as Tamlecht Muintre artholain, the â€laguegrave of
artholonâ€s eopleâ€. This would seem to have been a development of the
very oldest form of the legendâ€”which knew nothing of a plague, but

their soourn in reland, to the other world, whence they cameâ€”and is
probably due to the gradual euhemeriation of the ancient gods into
ancient men.

ollowing the race of artholon, came the race of emed, which carried

again enlarged herself, to the extent of twelve new plains and four more

remnant, left without their leader, were terribly oppressed by the

race of emed during the year were to be delivered up on each day of
amhain. oaded by this to a last desperate effort, the survivors of
emedâ€s people attacked the tower, and took it, onann perishing in the
struggle. ut their triumph was short. Morc, the other king, collected
his forces, and inflicted such a slaughter upon the people of emed

diedâ€”the two acts being, mythologically speaking, the same.

ne cannot help seeing a good deal of similarity between the stories of
these two mythical invasions of reland. specially noticeable is the
account of the epidemic which destroyed all artholonâ€s people and
nearly all of emedâ€s. ence it has been held that the two legends are

somewhat differently by two races, the berians and the aels. rofessor

These â€ir olgsâ€ are found in myth as the next coloniers of reland.

e have practically no other details regarding their life in reland. t
is obvious, however, that they were not really gods, but the preryan
race which the aels, when they landed in reland, found already in
occupation. There are many instances of peoples at a certain stage of
culture regarding tribes in a somewhat lower one as semidivine, or,
rather, halfdiabolical. The suspicion and fear with which the early
elts must have regarded the savage aborigines made them seem â€larger
than humanâ€. They feared them for the weird magical rites which they

The enemies alike of ir olg and omor, the Tuatha  anann, gods of

consent to this, the Tuatha  anann offered them a fifth part of
reland, whichever province they might choose. They agreed, and chose
onnaught, ever afterwards their especial home, and where, until the
middle of the seventeenth century, men were still found tracing their
descent from reng.

The whole story has a singularly historical, curiously unmythological
air about it, which contrasts strangely with the account of the other
battle of the same name which the Tuatha  anann waged afterwards with
the omors. The neighbourhood of ong still preserves both relics and
traditions of the fight. pon the plain of â€outhern Moyturaâ€ as it is
called, to distinguish it from the â€orthern Moyturaâ€ of the second
battle are many circles and tumuli. These circles are especially
numerous near the village itself and it is said that there were
formerly others, which have been used for making walls and dykes. Large
cairns of stones, too, are scattered over what was certainly once the
scene of a great battle. These various prehistoric monuments each
have their stilltold story and ir illiam ilde, as he relates in his
Lough orrib, was so impressed by the unexpected agreement
between the details of the legendary battle, as he read them in the
ancient manuscript, and the traditions still attaching to the mounds,
circles, and cairns, that he tells us he could not help coming to the
conclusion that the account was absolutely historical. ertainly the
coincidences are curious. is opinion was that the â€ir olgsâ€ were a
colony of elg, and that the â€Tuatha  anannâ€ were anes. ut the
people of the goddess anu are too obviously mythical to make it worth
while to seek any standingground for them in the world of reality. n
their superhuman attributes, they are uite different from the ir
olgs. n the epical cycle it is made as clear that the Tuatha  anann
are divine beings as it is that the ir olg, the ir omnann, and the
ir aillion stand on exactly the same footing as the men of lster.
Later history records by what Milesian kings and on what terms of
rackrent the three tribes were allowed settlements in other parts of
reland than their native onnaught. They appear in ancient, medival,
and almost modern chronicles as the old race of the land. The truth
seems to be that the whole story of the war between the gods and the ir
olgs is an invention of comparatively late times. n the earliest
documents there is only one battle of Moytura, fought between the people
of the goddess anu and the omors. The idea of doubling it seems to
date from after the eleventh century and its inventor may very
well have used the legends concerning this battlefield, where two
unknown armies had fought in days gone by, in compiling his story. t
never belonged to the same genuine mythological stratum as the legend of
the original battle fought by the Tuatha  anann, the gods of the
aels, against the omors, the gods of the berians.

This tale is evidently the work of some ancient rish storyteller who
wished to compile from various sources a more or less complete account

youth of the gods it was with horses and chariot that ran along

the arden of the esperides were surely of the same celestial growth as
those that fed the inhabitants of that immortal country while the
cookingspit reminds us of three such implements at Tara, made by
oibniu and associated with the names of the agda and the Morrg.

y this time the seven years of preparation had come to an end.  week
before the ay of amhain, the Morrg discovered that the omors had
landed upon rin. he at once sent a messenger to tell the agda, who
ordered his druids and sorcerers to go to the ford of the iver nius,
in ligo, and utter incantations against them.

The people of the goddess anu, however, were not yet uite ready for
battle. o the agda decided to visit the omorian camp as an

omors received him with apparent courtesy, and, to celebrate his
coming, prepared him a feast of porridge for it was wellknown how fond
he was of such food. They poured into their kingâ€s cauldron, which was
as deep as five giantâ€s fists, fourscore gallons of new milk, with meal
and bacon in proportion. To this they added the whole carcasses of
goats, sheep, and pigs they boiled the mixture together, and poured it
into a hole in the ground. â€ow,â€ said they, â€if you do not eat it all,
we shall put you to death, for we will not have you go back to your own
people and say that the omors are inhospitable.â€ ut they did not
succeed in frightening the agda. e took his spoon, which was so large
that two persons of our puny sie might have reclined comfortably in the
middle of it, dipped it into the porridge, and fished up halves of
salted pork and uarters of bacon.

â€f it tastes as good as it smells,â€ he said, â€it is good fare.â€ nd so
it proved for he ate it all, and scraped up even what remained at the
bottom of the hole. Then he went away to sleep it off, followed by the
laughter of the omors for his stomach was so swollen with food that he
could hardly walk. t was larger than the biggest cauldron in a large
house, and stood out like a sail before the wind.

ut the omorsâ€ little practical oke upon the agda had given the
Tuatha  anann time to collect their forces. t was on the eve of
amhain that the two armies came face to face. ven then the omors
could not believe that the people of the goddess anu would offer them
much resistance.

â€o you think they will really dare to give us battleâ€ said ress to
ndech, the son of omnu. â€f they do not pay their tribute, we will
pound their bones for them,â€ he replied.

The war of gods and giants naturally mirrored the warfare of the aels,
in whose battles, as in those of most semibarbarous people, single
combat figured largely. The main armies stood still, while, every day,
duels took place between ambitious combatants. ut no great warriors
either of the Tuatha  anann or of the omors took part in them.

ometimes a god, sometimes a giant would be the victor but there was a
difference in the net results that astonished the omors. f their own
swords and lances were broken, they were of no more use, and if their
own champions were killed, they never came back to life again but it
was uite otherwise with the people of the goddess anu. eapons
shattered on one day reappeared upon the next in as good condition as
though they had never been used, and warriors slain on one day came back
upon the morrow unhurt, and ready, if necessary, to be killed again.

The omors decided to send someone to discover the secret of these
prodigies. The spy they chose was uadan, the son of ress and of
rigit, daughter of the agda, and therefore halfgiant and halfgod. e
disguised himself as a Tuatha  anann warrior, and went to look for
oibniu. e found him at his forge, together with Luchtain, the
carpenter, and redn, the broneworker. e saw how oibniu forged
lanceheads with three blows of his hammer, while Luchtain cut shafts
for them with three blows of his axe, and redn fixed the two parts
together so adroitly that his brone nails needed no hammering in. e
went back and told the omors, who sent him again, this time to try and
kill oibniu.

e reappeared at the forge, and asked for a avelin. ithout suspicion,
oibniu gave him one, and, as soon as he got it into his hand, he thrust
it through the smithâ€s body. ut oibniu plucked it out, and, hurling it
back at his assailant, mortally wounded him. uadan went home to die,

for the purpose the rish â€keeningâ€. oibniu, on the other hand, took no
harm. e went to the physician iancecht, who, with his daughter irmid,

henever one of the Tuatha  anann was killed or wounded, he was
brought to the two doctors, who plunged him into the wonderworking
water, and brought him back to life and health again.

The mystic spring was not long, however, allowed to help the people of
the goddess.  young omorian chief, ctriallach son of ndech, found it

carrying a large stone from the bed of the iver rowes. These they
dropped into the spring, until they had filled it, dispersed the healing
water, and formed a cairn above it. Legend has identified this place by
the name of the â€airn of ctriallachâ€.

This success determined the omors to fight a pitched battle. They drew

ll the great fighters of the Tuatha  anann were drawn out opposite

The omors saw him too, and marvelled. â€t seems wonderful to me,â€

Then the two armies charged each other with a great shout. pears and

seemed as if thunder rolled everywhere.

They fought so closely that the heads, hands, and feet of those on one

Many great chiefs fell on each side. gma, the champion of the Tuatha

n ancient poem has handed down the secret of this magic stone. t is
there called a tathlum, meaning a â€concrete ballâ€ such as the ancient

             â€ tathlum, heavy, fiery, firm,
              hich the Tuatha  anann had with them,
              t was that broke the fierce alorâ€s eye,
              f old, in the battle of the great armies.

             â€The blood of toads and furious bears,
              nd the blood of the noble lion,
              The blood of vipers and of smuinnâ€s trunksâ€”
              t was of these the tathlum was composed.

structures of almost every kind are found among themâ€”stone cairns with

perhaps the most impressive of all primeval ruins. hat they really
commemorated we may never know, but, in all probability, the place was

it. Those which have been examined were found to contain burnt wood and
the halfburnt bones of men and horses, as well as implements of flint
and bone. The actors, therefore, were still in the eolithic ge.
hether the horses were domesticated ones buried with their riders, or
wild ones eaten at the funeral feasts, it would be hard to decide. The
history of the real event must have been long lost even at the early
date when its relics were pointed out as the records of a battle between
the gods and the giants of aelic myth.

f what adb had in mind when she uttered this prophecy we have no
record. ut it was true. The twilight of the rish gods was at hand. 
new race was coming across the sea to dispute the ownership of reland
with the people of the goddess anu. nd these newcomers were not
divinities like themselves, but men like ourselves, ancestors of the
aels.

This story of the conuest of the gods by mortalsâ€”which seems such a
strange one to usâ€”is typically eltic. The aelic mythology is the only
one which has preserved it in any detail but the doctrine would seem to
have been common at one time to all the elts. t was, however, of less
shame to the gods than would otherwise have been for men were of as
divine descent as themselves. The dogma of the elts was that men were
descended from the god of death, and first came from the Land of the
ead to take possession of the present world. aesar tells us, in
his too short account of the auls, that they believed themselves to be
sprung from is ater, the god of the underworld. n the aelic
mythology is ater was called il, a name which has for root the
syllable bel, meaning â€to dieâ€. The god eli in ritish mythology was
no doubt the same person, while the same idea is expressed by the same
root in the name of alor, the terrible omor whose glance was
death.

The posthristian rish chroniclers, seeking to reconcile hristian
teachings with the still vital pagan mythology by changing the gods into
ancient kings and incorporating them into the annals of the country,
with appropriate dates, also disposed of the genuine early doctrine by
substituting pain for ades, and giving a highlyfanciful account of
the origin and wanderings of their ancestors. To use a ibernicism,
appropriate in this connection, the first rishman was a cythian called
enius arsa. eprived of his own throne, he had settled in gypt, where
his son iul married a daughter of the reigning haraoh. er name was
cta, and she had a son called oidel, whose greatgrandson was named
ber cot, the whole genealogy being probably invented to explain the
origin of the three names by which the aels called themselvesâ€”inn,
cot, and oidel. enius and his family and clan were turned out of
gypt for refusing to oin in the persecution of the children of srael,
and soourned in frica for fortytwo years. Their wanderings took them
to â€the altars of the hilistines, by the Lake of siersâ€ then, passing
between usicada and the hilly country of yria, they travelled through
Mauretania as far as the illars of ercules and thence landed in
pain, where they lived many years, greatly increasing and multiplying.
The same route is given by the twelfthcentury ritish historian,
eoffrey of Monmouth, as that taken by rutus and the Troans when they
came to colonie ritain. ts only connection with any kind of fact
is that it corresponds fairly well with what ethnologists consider must
have been the westward line of migration taken, not, curiously enough,
by the ryan elts, but by the preryan berians.

t is sufficient for us to find the first men in pain, remembering that
â€painâ€ stood for the eltic ades, or lysium. n this country regon,

â€Terrestrialâ€ they may have been in form, but their acts were

The heroic age of reland was not, however, the mere orgy of battle
which one might assume from the previous chapter. t had room for its
