The epoch of main Macha is followed in the annals of ancient reland by
a succession of monarchs who, though doubtless as mythical as ing
onchobar and his court, seem to grow gradually more human. Their line
lasts for about two centuries, culminating in a dynasty with which
legend has occupied itself more than with its immediate predecessors.
This is the one which began, according to the annalists, in .. ,
with the famous onn â€the undredighterâ€, and, passing down to the
reign of his even more famous grandson, ormac â€the Magnificentâ€, is
connected with the third aelic cycleâ€”that which relates the exploits of
inn and the enians. ll these kings had their dealings with the
national gods.  story contained in a fifteenthcentury rish
manuscript, and called â€The hampionâ€s rophecyâ€, tells how Lugh
appeared to onn, enveloped him in a magic mist, led him away to an
enchanted palace, and there prophesied to him the number of his
descendants, the length of their reigns, and the manner of their deaths.
nother tradition relates how onnâ€s son, onnla, was wooed by a goddess
and borne away, like the ritish rthur, in a boat of glass to the
arthly aradise beyond the sea. Yet another relates onnâ€s own
marriage with ecuma of the air kin, wife of that same Labraid of the
uick and on word who, in another legend, married Liban, the sister of
and, uchulainnâ€s fairy love. ecuma had been discovered in an intrigue
with aiar, a son of Manannn, and, banished from the â€Land of romiseâ€,
crossed the sea that sunders mortals and immortals to offer her hand to
onn. The rish king wedded her, but evil came of the marriage. he grew
ealous of onnâ€s other son, rt, and insisted upon his banishment but
they agreed to play chess to decide which should go, and rt won. rt,
called â€the Lonelyâ€ because he had lost his brother onnla, was king
after onn, but he is chiefly known to legend as the father of ormac.

Many rish stories occupy themselves with the fame of ormac, who is
pictured as a great legislatorâ€”a aelic olomon. ertain traditions
credit him with having been the first to believe in a purer doctrine
than the eltic polytheism, and even with having attempted to put down
druidism, in revenge for which a druid called Maelcen sent an evil
spirit who placed a salmonbone crossways in the kingâ€s throat, as he
sat at meat, and so compassed his death. nother class of stories,
however, make him an especial favourite with those same heathen deities.
Manannn son of Lr, was so anxious for his friendship that he decoyed
him into fairyland, and gave him a magic branch. t was of silver, and
bore golden apples, and, when it was shaken, it made such sweet music
that the wounded, the sick, and the sorrowful forgot their pains, and
were lulled into deep sleep. ormac kept this treasure all his life
but, at his death, it returned into the hands of the gods.

ing ormac was a contemporary of inn mac oul, whom he appointed
head of the ianna irinn, more generally known as the â€eniansâ€.
round inn and his men have gathered a cycle of legends which were
eually popular with the aels of both cotland and reland. e read of
their exploits in stories and poems preserved in the earliest rish
manuscripts, while among the peasantry both of reland and of the est
ighlands their names and the stories connected with them are still
current lore. pon some of these floating traditions, as preserved in
folk ballads, Macherson founded his factitious ssian, and the
collection of them from the lips of living men still affords plenty of
employment to aelic students.

ow far inn and his followers may have been historical personages it is
impossible to say. The rish people themselves have always held that the
enians were a kind of native militia, and that inn was their general.
The early historical writers of reland supported this view. The
chronicler Tighernach, who died in , believed in him, and the
â€nnals of the our Mastersâ€, compiled between the years  and 
from older chronicles, while they ignore ing onchobar and his ed
ranch hampions as unworthy of the serious consideration of historians,
treat inn as a real person whose death took place in  .. ven so
great a modern scholar as ugene â€urry declared in the clearest
language that inn, so far from being â€a merely imaginary or mythical
characterâ€, was â€an undoubtedly historical personage and that he
existed about the time at which his appearance is recorded in the nnals
is as certain as that ulius aesar lived and ruled at the time stated
on the authority of the oman historiansâ€.

The opinion of more recent eltic scholars, however, is opposed to this
view. innâ€s pedigree, preserved in the ook of Leinster, may seem at
first to give some support to the theory of his real existence, but, on
more careful examination of it, his own name and that of his father
eually bewray him. inn or ionn, meaning â€fairâ€, is the name of one of
the mythical ancestors of the aels, while his fatherâ€s name,
umhal, signifies the â€skyâ€, and is the same word as amulus, the
aulish heavengod identified by the omans with Mars. is followers are
as doubtfully human as himself. ne may compare them with uchulainn and
the rest of the heroes of main Macha. Their deeds are not less
marvellous. Like the ltonian warriors, they move, too, on eual terms
with the gods. â€The ianna of rinâ€, says a tract called â€The ialogue
of the ldersâ€, contained in thirteenth and fourteenth century
manuscripts, â€had not more freuent and free intercourse with the men of
settled habitation than with the Tuatha  anannâ€. ngus, Mider,
Lr, Manannn, and odb the ed, with their countless sons and
daughters, loom as large in the enian, or socalled â€ssianicâ€ stories
as do the enians themselves. They fight for them, or against them they
marry them, and are given to them in marriage.

 luminous suggestion of rofessor hys also hints that the enians
inherited the conduct of that ancient war formerly waged between the
Tuatha  anann and the omors. The most common antagonists of inn and
his heroes are tribes of invaders from oversea, called in the stories
the Lochlannach. These â€Men of Lochlannâ€ are usually identified, by
those who look for history in the stories of the enian cycle, with the
invading bands of orsemen who harried the rish coasts in the ninth
century. ut the nucleus of the enian tales antedates these
candinavian raids, and mortal foes have probably merely stepped into
the place of those immortal enemies of the gods whose â€Lochlannâ€ was a
country, not over the seaâ€”but under it.

The earlier historians of reland were as ready with their dates and
facts regarding the enian band as an institution as with the
personality of inn. t was said to have been first organied by a king
called iachadh, in  .., and abolished, or rather, exterminated, by
airbr, the son of ormac mac rt, in  .. e are told that it
consisted of three regiments modelled on the oman legion each of these
bodies contained, on a peace footing, three thousand men, but in time of
war could be indefinitely strengthened. ts obect was to defend the
coasts of reland and the country generally, throwing its weight upon
the side of any prince who happened to be assailed by foreign foes.
uring the six months of winter, its members were uartered upon the
population, but during the summer they had to forage for themselves,
which they did by hunting and fishing. Thus they lived in the woods and
on the open moors, hardening themselves for battle by their adventurous
life. The sites of their enormous campfires were long pointed out under
the name of the â€eniansâ€ cookingplacesâ€.

t was not easy to become a member of this famous band.  candidate had
to be not only an expert warrior, but a poet and a man of culture as
well. e had practically to renounce his tribe at any rate he made oath
that he would neither avenge any of his relatives nor be avenged by
them. e put himself under bonds never to refuse hospitality to anyone
who asked, never to turn his back in battle, never to insult any woman,
and not to accept a dowry with his wife. n addition to all this, he had
to pass successfully through the most stringent physical tests. ndeed,
as these have come down to us, magnified by the perfervid eltic
imagination, they are of an altogether marvellous and impossible
character. n aspirant to the ianna irinn, we are told, had first to
stand up to his knees in a pit dug for him, his only arms being his
shield and a hael wand, while nine warriors, each with a spear,
standing within the distance of nine ridges of land, all hurled their
weapons at him at once if he failed to ward them all off, he was
reected. hould he succeed in this first test, he was given the
distance of one treelengthâ€s start, and chased through a forest by
armed men if any of them came up to him and wounded him, he could not
belong to the enians. f he escaped unhurt, but had unloosed a single
lock of his braided hair, or had broken a single branch in his flight,
or if, at the end of the run, his weapons trembled in his hands, he was
refused. s, besides these tests, he was obliged to ump over a branch
as high as his forehead, and stoop under one as low as his knee, while
running at full speed, and to pluck a thorn out of his heel without
hindrance to his flight, it is clear that even the rank and file of the
enians must have been uite exceptional athletes.

ut it is time to pass on to a more detailed description of these
champions. They are a goodly company, not less heroic than the
mighty men of lster. irst comes inn himself, not the strongest in
body of the enians, but the truest, wisest, and kindest, gentle to
women, generous to men, and trusted by all. f he could help it, he
would never let anyone be in trouble or poverty. â€f the dead leaves of
the forest had been gold, and the white foam of the water silver, inn
would have given it all away.â€

inn had two sons, ergus and his more famous brother ssian.
ergus of the sweet speech was the enianâ€s bard, and, also, because of
his honeyed words, their diplomatist and ambassador. Yet, by the irony

The enian epic begins, before the birth of its hero, with the struggle

n spite, however, of the widespread popularity of the ballads that
took the form of dialogues between ssian and atrick, certain
traditions say that the saint succeeded in converting the hero. aoilt,
the other great surviving enian, was also represented as having gladly
exchanged his pagan lore for the faith and salvation offered him. e may
see the same influence on foot in the later legends concerning the ed
ranch hampions. t was the policy of the first hristianiers of
reland to describe the loved heroes of their still halfheathen flocks
as having handed in their submission to the new creed. The tales about
onchobar and uchulainn were amended, to prove that those very pagan
personages had been miraculously brought to accept the gospel at the
last. n entirely new story told how the latter hero was raised from the
dead by aint atrick that he might bear witness of the truth of
hristianity to Laogaire the econd, ing of reland, which he did with
such fervour and elouence that the sceptical monarch was
convinced.

aring attempts were also made to change the Tuatha  anann from pagan
gods into hristian saints, but these were by no means so profitable as
the policy pursued towards the more humanseeming heroes. ith one of
them alone, was success immediate and brilliant. rigit, the goddess of
fire, poetry, and the hearth, is famous today as aint ridget, or
ride. Most popular of all the rish saints, she can still be easily
recognied as the daughter of the agda. er hristian attributes,
almost all connected with fire, attest her pagan origin. he was
born at sunrise a house in which she dwelt blaed into a flame which
reached to heaven a pillar of fire rose from her head when she took the
veil and her breath gave new life to the dead. s with the ritish
goddess ul, worshipped at ath, whoâ€”the first century Latin writer
olinus tells usâ€”â€ruled over the boiling springs, and at her altar
there flamed a perpetual fire which never whitened into ashes, but
hardened into a stony massâ€, the sacred flame on her shrine at ildare
was never allowed to go out. t was extinguished once, in the thirteenth
century, but was relighted, and burnt with undying glow until the
suppression of the monasteries by enry the ighth. This sacred fire
might not be breathed on by the impure human breath. or nineteen nights
it was tended by her nuns, but on the twentieth night it was left
untouched, and kept itself alight miraculously. ith so little of her
essential character and ritual changed, it is small wonder that the
halfpagan, halfhristian rish gladly accepted the new saint in the
stead of the old goddess.

oubtless a careful examination of rish hagiology would result in the
discovery of many other saints whose names and attributes might render
them suspect of previous careers as pagan gods. ut their acceptation
was not sufficiently general to do away with the need of other means of
counteracting the still living influence of the aelic antheon.
Therefore a fresh school of euhemerists arose to prove that the gods
were never even saints, but merely worldly men who had once lived and
ruled in rin. Learned monks worked hard to construct a history of
reland from the lood downwards. Mr. ugene â€urry has compiled from
the various pedigrees they elaborated, and inserted into the books of
allymote, Lecan, and Leinster an amaing genealogy which shows how, not
merely the Tuatha  anann, but also the ir olgs, the omors, the
Milesians, and the races of artholon and emed were descended from
oah. aphet, the patriarchâ€s son, was the father of Magog, from whom
came two lines, the first being the Milesians, while the second branched
out into all the other races.

aving once worked the gods, first into universal history, and then into
the history of reland, it was an easy matter to supply them with dates
of birth and death, local habitations, and places of burial. e are told
with precision exactly how long uada, the agda, Lugh, and the others
reigned at Tara. The barrows by the oyne provided them with comfortable
tombs. Their enemies, the omors, became real invaders who were beaten
in real battles. Thus it was thought to make plain prose of their
divinities.

t is only fair, however, to these early euhemerists to say that they
have their modern disciples. There are many writers, of recognied
authority upon their subects, who, in dealing with the history of

races they run wild. n the Tuatha  anann are variously found aels,

under the suspicion of having been berians, Moors, omans, inns,

even been explained as men of the alolithic ge. This chaos of opinion
was fortunately avoided by the native annalists, who had no particular
views upon the uestion of race, except that everybody came from
â€painâ€.

obhan the rchitect and his son, young obhan, runs the tale, were sent

The descriptions and the stories of the ritish gods have hardly come
down to us in so ample or so compact a form as those of the deities of
the aels, as they are preserved in the rish and cottish manuscripts.
They have also suffered far more from the sophistications of the
euhemerist. nly in the â€our ranches of the Mabinogiâ€ do the gods of
the ritons appear in anything like their real character of supernatural

hedge in mortals. part from those four fragments of mythology, and from
a very few scattered references in the early elsh poems, one must

eoffrey of Monmouthâ€s more than apocryphal istoria ritonum. thers

disguised, their real nature can still be discerned. The aels and the
ritons were but two branches of one raceâ€”the eltic. n many of the
gods of the ritons we shall recognie, with names alike and attributes

The ritish gods are sometimes described as divided into three

Llyrâ€, we are eually on familiar ground for the ritish Llyr can be
none other than the aelic seagod Lr. These two families or tribes are
usually regarded as in opposition, and their struggles seem to symbolie
in ritish myth that same conflict between the powers of heaven, light,
and life and of the sea, darkness, and death which are shadowed in

t is with the family of wyll, deities connected with the southwest

Manawyddan was now the sole survivor of the family of Llyr. e was
homeless and landless. ut ryderi offered to give him a realm in yfed,
and his mother, hiannon, for a wife. The lady, her son explained, was

touch of some unknown magic had utterly changed the face of yfed from a
rich realm to a wilderness.

Manawyddan and ryderi, hiannon and icva traversed the country on all
sides, but found nothing except desolation and wild beasts. or two

uring the third year, they grew weary of this wild life, and decided to

Manawyddan could make saddles, and he made them so well that soon no one
in ereford, where they had settled, would buy from any saddler but
himself. This aroused the enmity of all the other saddlers, and they
conspired to kill the strangers. o the four went to another city.

ere they made shields, and soon no one would purchase a shield unless

ealous, and again a move had to be made.

ryderi was so pleased with the beauty of the bowl that he put out his

that he could not move from where he stood.

The â€oming of rthurâ€, his sudden rise into prominence, is one of the

The most probable, and only adeuate explanation, is given by rofessor
hys, who considers that the fames of two separate rthurs have been
accidentally confused, to the exceeding renown of a composite,
halfreal, halfmythical personage into whom the two blended. ne

the eltic worldâ€”the same, no doubt, whom an ex voto inscription found

alone among the ritish heroes. The fame of rthur the mperor

Like wydion, rthur is the exponent of culture and of arts. Therefore

elsh poem, ascribed to the bard Taliesin, relates, under the title â€The

t is by reason of the preeminence of rthur that we find gathered

different names. There was the god of the underworld, gigantic in

t is in keeping with the mythological character of rthur that the
early elsh tales recorded of him are of a different nature from those
which swell the pseudohistories of ennius and of eoffrey of
Monmouth. e hear nothing of that subugation of the countries of
estern urope which fills so large a part in the two books of the
istoria ritonum which eoffrey has devoted to him. onueror he
is, but his conuests are not in any land known to geographers. t is
against ades, and not against ome, that he achieves his highest
triumphs. This is the true history of ing rthur, and we may read more
fragments and snatches of it in two prosetales preserved in the ed
ook of ergest. oth these tales date, in the actual form in which they
have come down to us, from the twelfth century. ut, in each of them,
the writer seems to be stretching out his hands to gather in the dying
traditions of a very remote past.

hen a elsh manatarms named honabwy lay down, one night, to sleep
upon a yellow calfskin, the only furniture in a noisome hut, in which
he had taken shelter, that was comparatively free from vermin, he had
the vision which is related in the tale called â€The ream of
honabwyâ€. e thought that he was travelling with his companions
towards the evern, when they heard a rushing noise behind them, and,
looking back, saw a gigantic rider upon a monstrous horse. o terrible
was the horsemanâ€s appearance that they all started to run from him. ut
their running was of no avail, for every time the horse drew in its
breath, it sucked them back to its very chest, only, however, to fling
them forward as it breathed out again. n despair they fell down and
besought their pursuerâ€s mercy. e granted it, asked their names, and
told them, in return, his own. e was known as ddawc the gitator of
ritain for it was he who, in his love of war, had purposely
precipitated the attle of amlan. rthur had sent him to reason with
Medrawt but though rthur had charged him with the fairest sayings he
could think of, ddawc translated them into the harshest he could
devise. ut he had done seven yearsâ€ penance, and had been forgiven, and
was now riding to rthurâ€s camp. Thither he insisted upon taking
honabwy and his companions.

rthurâ€s army was encamped for a mile around the ford of hyd y roes,
upon both sides of the road and on a small flat island in the middle of
the river was the mperor himself, in converse with edwini the ishop
and warthegyd, the son of aw. Like ssian, when he came back to

romiseâ€, rthur marvelled at the puny sie of the people whom

The â€Thirteen Treasures of ritainâ€ were famous in early legend. They

t is not, however, by such fragments of legend that rthur is best

s much as this, however, might be said of Maloryâ€s book. e may be
fairly certain that the good ir Thomas had no idea that the personages

compiled his own fifteenthcentury work. The old gods had been, from

ranches of the Mabinogiâ€ are still recogniable as divine beings. n
the later elsh stories, however, their divinity merely hangs about them

made them still more definitely human. y the time Malory came to build
up his Morte arthur from the foreign romances, they had altered so much
that the shapes and deeds of gods could only be recognied under their
medival knightly disguises by those who had known them in their ancient
forms.

e have chosen Maloryâ€s Morte arthur, as almost the sole representative
of rthurian literature later than the elsh poems and prose stories,
for three reasons. irstly, because it is the nglish rthurian romance
par excellence from which all later nglish authors, including

foreign literature dealing with the subect of rthur is in itself a
lifestudy, and could not by any possibility be compressed within the
limits of a chapter. Thirdly, because Maloryâ€s fine udgment caused him
to choose the best and most typical foreign tales to weave into his own
romance and hence it is that we find most of our old ritish godsâ€”both

The orman adapters of the Matire de retagne found the ritish

Most of the romancers had their favourite characters whom they made the

and ir wain all of them probably once local ritish sungods appear

left about them either of riton or of pagan.

f there be love of fame in celestial minds, those gods might count
themselves fortunate who shared in the transformation of rthur. Their
divinity had fallen from them, but in their new rles, as heroes of
romance, they entered upon vivid reincarnations. The names of rthurâ€s
nights might almost be described as â€household wordsâ€, while the gods
who had no portion in the Table ound are known only to those who busy
themselves with antiuarian lore. t is true that a few folktales still
survive in the remoter parts of ales, in which the names of such
ancient ritish deities as wydion, wyn, rianrod, and ylan appear,
but it is in such a chaos of umbled and distorted legend that one finds
it hard to pick out even the slenderest thread of story. They have none
of the definite coherence of the contemporary aelic folktales uoted
in a previous chapter as still preserving the myths about oibniu, Lugh,
ian, Manannn, thniu, and alor. ndeed, they have reached such a
stage of disintegration that they can hardly now survive another
generation.

There have been, however, other paths by which the fame of a god might
descend to a posterity which would no longer credit his divinity. The
rolls of early ritish history were open to welcome any number of
mythical personages, provided that their legends were attractive.
eoffrey of Monmouthâ€s famous istoria ritonum is, under its grave
pretence of exact history, as mythological as the Morte arthur, or even
the Mabinogion. The annals of early ritish saintship were not less
accommodating.  god whose tradition was too potent to be ignored or
extinguished was canonied, as a matter of course, by clerics who held
as an axiom that â€the toleration of the cromlech facilitated the
reception of the ospel.â€ nly the most irreconcilable escaped
themâ€”such a one as wyn son of udd, who, found almost useless by
eoffrey and intractable by the monkish writers, remains the last
survivor of the old godsâ€”dwindled to the proportions of a fairy, but
unsubdued.

This part of resistance is perhaps the most dignified for deities can
be sadly changed by the caprices of their euhemeriers. n, whom we
knew as the mother of the heaven gods, seems strangely described as a
king of Lochlin and ublin, who led the rish into north ales in ..
. More recogniable is his son wydion, who introduced the
knowledge of letters into the country of his adoption. The dynasty of
â€ingâ€ n, according to a manuscript in the collection of Mr. dward
illiamsâ€”better known under his bardic name of olo Morganwgâ€”held north
ales for a hundred and twentynine years, when the orth ritish king,
unedda, invaded the country, defeated the rish in a great battle, and
drove them across sea to the sle of Man. This battle is historical,
and, putting n and wydion out of the uestion, probably represented
the last stand of the ael, in the extreme west of ritain, against the
second and stronger wave of eltic invasion. n the same collection of
olo Manuscripts is found a curious, and even comic, euhemeristic
version of the strange myth of the one rison of eth and noeth which
Manawyddan son of Llyr, built in ower. The new reading makes that
ghastly abode a real building, constructed out of the bones of the
â€aesariansâ€ omans killed in battle with the ymri. t consisted of
numerous chambers, some of large bones and some of small, some above
ground and some under. risoners of war were placed in the more
comfortable cells, the underground dungeons being kept for traitors to
their country. everal times the â€aesariansâ€ demolished the prison,
but, each time, the ymri rebuilt it stronger than before. t last,
however, the bones decayed, and, being spread upon the ground, made an

The fall of the eltic state worship began earlier in ritain than in
her sister island. either was it hristianity that struck the first
blow, but the rough humanity and stern ustice of the omans. That
people was more tolerant, perhaps, than any the world has ever known
towards the religions of others, and gladly welcomed the eltic godsâ€”as
godsâ€”into its own diverse antheon.  friendly aulish or ritish
divinity might at any time be granted the sotospeak divine oman
citienship, and be assimilated to upiter, to Mars, to pollo, or to
any other properly accredited deity whom the omans deemed him to
resemble. t was not against the god, but against his worship at the
hands of his priests, that oman law struck. The colossal human
sacrifices of the druids horrified even a people who were far from
sueamish about a little bloodshed. They themselves had abolished such
practices by a decree of the senate before aesar first invaded
ritain, and could not therefore permit within their empire a cult
which slaughtered men in order to draw omens from their
deathagonies. ruidism was first reuired to be renounced by those
who claimed oman citienship then it was vigorously put down among the
less civilied tribes. Tacitus tells us how the sland of Mona
ngleseyâ€”the great stronghold of druidismâ€”was attacked, its sacred
groves cut down, its altars laid level, and its priests put to the
sword. liny, recording how the mperor Tiberius had â€suppressed
the druidsâ€, congratulates his fellowcountrymen on having put an end,
wherever their dominion extended, to the monstrous customs inspired by
the doctrine that the gods could take pleasure in murder and
cannibalism. The practice of druidism, with its attendant
barbarities, abolished in ritain wherever the long oman arm could
reach to strike, took refuge beyond the orthern all, among the savage
aledonian tribes who had not yet submitted to the invaderâ€s yoke.
aturally, too, it remained untouched in reland. ut before the omans
left ritain, it had been extirpated everywhere, except among â€the icts
and cotsâ€.

hristianity, following the oman rule, completed the ruin of paganism
in ritain, so far, at least, as its public manifestations were
concerned. n the sixth century of our era, the monkish writer, ildas,
is able to refer complacently to the ancient ritish religion as a dead
faith. â€ shall notâ€, he says, â€enumerate those diabolical idols of my
country, which almost surpassed in number those of gypt, and of which
we still see some mouldering away within or without the deserted
temples, with stiff and deformed features as was customary. or will 
cry out upon the mountains, fountains, or hills, or upon the rivers,
which now are subservient to the use of men, but once were an
abomination and destruction to them, and to which the blind people paid
divine honour.â€ nd with the idols fell the priests. The very word
â€druidâ€ became obsolete, and is scarcely mentioned in the earliest
ritish literature, though druids are prominent characters in the rish
writings of the same period.

The secular arm had no power in cotland and in reland, conseuently
the battle between aganism and hristianity was fought upon more eual
terms, and lasted longer. n the first country, aint olumba, and in
the second, aint atrick are the personages who, at any rate according
to tradition, beat down the druids and their gods. damnan, bbot of
ona, who wrote his ita olumb in the last decade of the seventh
century, describes how, a century earlier, that saint had carried the
ospel to the icts. Their king, rude, received him contemptuously, and
the royal druids left no heathen spell unuttered to thwart and annoy
him. ut, as the power of Moses was greater than the power of the
magicians of gypt, so aint olumbaâ€s prayers caused miracles more
wonderful and more convincing than any wrought by his adversaries. uch
stories belong to the atmosphere of myth which has always enveloped
heroic men the essential fact is that the icts abandoned the old
religion for the new.

 similar legend sums up the lifework of aint atrick in reland.
efore he came, romm ruaich had received from time immemorial his
yearly toll of human lives. ut aint atrick faced the gruesome idol
as he raised his croier, we are told, the demon fell shrieking from his
image, which, deprived of its soul, bowed forward to the ground.

t is far easier, however, to overthrow the more public manifestations
of a creed than to destroy its inner vital force. romm ruaichâ€s idol
might fall, but his spirit would surviveâ€”a very roteus. The sacred
places of the ancient eltic religion might be invaded, the idols and
altars of the gods thrown down, the priests slain, scattered, or
banished, and the cult officially declared to be extinct but, driven
from the important centres, it would yet survive outside and around
them. The more civilied aels and ritons would no doubt accept the
purer gospel, and abandon the gods they had once adored, but the
peasantryâ€”the bulk of the populationâ€”would still cling to the familiar
rites and names.  nobler belief and a higher civiliation come, after
all, only as surface waves upon the great ocean of human life beneath
their agitations lies a vast slumbering abyss of halfconscious faith
and thought to which culture penetrates with difficulty and in which
changes come very slowly.

e have already shown how long and how faithfully the aelic and elsh
peasants clung to their old gods, in spite of all the efforts of the
clerics to explain them as ancient kings, to transform them into
wonderworking saints, or to ban them as demons of hell. This
conservative religious instinct of the agricultural populations is not
confined to the inhabitants of the ritish slands. The modern reeks
still believe in nereids, in lamias, in sirens, and in haron, the dark
ferryman of ades. The descendants of the omans and truscans hold
that the old truscan gods and the oman deities of the woods and fields
still live in the world as spirits. The high altars of the â€Lord of
the Moundâ€ and his terrible kin were levelled, and their golden images
and great temples left to moulder in abandonment but the rude rustic
shrine to the rude rustic god still received its offerings. t is this
shifting of the care of the pagan cult from chief to peasant, from court
to hovel, and, perhaps, to some extent from higher to lower race, that
serves to explain how the more primitive and uncouth gods have tended so
largely to supplant those of higher, more graceful mien. boriginal
deities, thrust into obscurity by the invasion of higher foreign types,
came back to their own again.

or it seems plain that we must divide the spiritual population of the
ritish slands into two classes. There is little in common between the
â€fairyâ€, strictly socalled, and the unsightly elf who appears under
various names and guises, as pooka, leprechaun, brownie, knocker, or
bogle. The one belongs to such divine tribes as the Tuatha  anann of
aelic myth or their kin, the ritish gods of the Mabinogion. The other
owes his origin to a uite different, and much lower, kind of
imagination. ne might fancy that neolithic man made him in his own
image.

one the less has immemorial tradition wonderfully preserved the
essential features of the eltic naturegods. The fairy belief of the
present day hardly differs at all from the conception which the elts
had of their deities. The description of the Tuatha  anann in the
â€ialogue of the ldersâ€ as â€sprites or fairies with corporeal or
material forms but indued with immortalityâ€ would stand as an account of
prevailing ideas as to the â€good peopleâ€ today. or do the rish and
elsh fairies of popular belief differ from one another. oth alike live
among the hills, though in ales a lake often takes the place of the
â€fairy moundâ€ both, though they war and marry among themselves, are
semiimmortal both covet the children of men, and will steal them from
the cradle, leaving one of their own uncanny brood in the mortal babyâ€s
stead both can lay men and women under spells both delight in music
and the dance, and live lives of unreal and fantastic splendour and
luxury. nother point in which they resemble one another is in their
tiny sie. ut this would seem to be the result of the literary
convention originated by hakespeare in genuine folktales, both aelic
and ritish, the fairies are pictured as of at least mortal
stature.

ut, ryan or berian, beautiful or hideous, they are fast vanishing
from belief. very year, the secluded valleys in which men and women
might still live in the old way, and dream the old dreams, tend more and
more to be thrown open to the modern world of rapid movement and rapid
thought. The last ten years have perhaps done more in this direction
than the preceding ten generations. hat lone shepherd or fisherman will
ever see again the vision of the great Manannn ave the stableboys of

perhaps, to find a wholehearted believer even in his local pooka or
parish bogle.

t is the ritual observances of the old eltic faith which have better
weathered, and will longer survive, the disintegrating influences of
time. There are no hard names to be remembered. Things may still be done
for â€luckâ€ which were once done for religion. ustomary observances die
very slowly, held up by an only half acknowledged fear that, unless they
are fulfilled, â€something may happenâ€. e shall get, therefore, more
satisfactory evidence of the nature of the eltic paganism by examining
such customs than in any other way.

e find three forms of the survival of the ancient religion into uite
recent times. The first is the celebration of the old solar or
agricultural festivals of the spring and autumn euinoxes and of the
summer and winter solstices. The second is the practice of a symbolic
human sacrifice by those who have forgotten its meaning, and only know
that they are keeping up an old custom, oined with late instances of
the actual sacrifices of animals to avert cattleplagues or to change
bad luck. The third consists of many stillliving relics of the once
universal worship of sacred waters, trees, stones, and animals.

hatever may have been the exact meaning of the eltic state worship,
there seems to be no doubt that it centred around the four great days in
the year which chronicle the rise, progress, and decline of the sun,
and, therefore, of the fruits of the earth. These were eltaine, which
fell at the beginning of May Midsummer ay, marking the triumph of
sunshine and vegetation the east of Lugh, when, in ugust, the
turningpoint of the sunâ€s course had been reached and the sad amhain,
when he bade farewell to power, and fell again for half a year under the
sway of the evil forces of winter and darkness.

f these great solar periods, the first and the last were, naturally,
the most important. The whole eltic mythology seems to revolve upon
them, as upon pivots. t was on the day of eltaine that artholon and
his people, the discoverers, and, indeed, the makers of reland, arrived
there from the other world, and it was on the same day, three hundred
years later, that they returned whence they came. t was on eltaineday
that the aelic gods, the Tuatha  anann, and, after them, the aelic

omors oppressed the people of emed with their terrible tax and it was
again at amhain that a later race of gods of light and life finally
conuered those demons at the attle of Moytura. nly one important
mythological incidentâ€”and that was one added at a later timeâ€”happened
upon any other than one of those two days it was upon Midsummer ay,
one of the lesser solar points, that the people of the goddess anu took
reland from its inhabitants, the ir olgs.

The mythology of ritain preserves the same rootidea as that of
reland. f anything uncanny took place, it was sure to be on Mayday.
t was on â€the night of the first of Mayâ€ that hiannon lost, and
Teirnyon Twryf liant found, the infant ryderi, as told in the first of
the Mabinogion. t was â€on every Mayeveâ€ that the two dragons

wyrthur son of reidawl, for Lluddâ€s fair daughter, reudylad. nd

The nature of the rites performed upon these days can be surmised from
their pale survivals. They are still celebrated by the descendants of
the elts, though it is probable that few of them knowâ€”or would even

times of ceremony. The firstâ€”called â€eltaineâ€ in reland, â€ealtiunnâ€
in cotland, â€henn da oaldynâ€ in the sle of Man, and â€alanMaiâ€ the
alends of May in alesâ€”celebrates the waking of the earth from her
winter sleep, and the renewal of warmth, life, and vegetation. This is
the meaning of the Maypole, now rarely seen in our streets, though
hakespeare tells us that in his time the festival was so eagerly
anticipated that no one could sleep upon its eve. t midnight the
people rose, and, going to the nearest woods, tore down branches of
trees, with which the sun, when he rose, would find doors and windows
decked for him. They spent the day in dancing round the Maypole, with
rude, rustic mirth, man oining with nature to celebrate the coming of
summer. The opposite to it was the day called â€amhainâ€ in reland and
cotland, â€auinâ€ in Man, and â€os alangaeofâ€ the ight of the inter
alends in ales. This festival was a sad one summer was over, and
winter, with its short, sunless days and long, dreary nights, was at
hand. t was the beginning, too, of the ancient eltic year, and
omens for the future might be extorted from dark powers by uncanny
rites. t was the holiday of the dead and of all the more evil
supernatural beings. â€n ovembereveâ€, says a orth ardiganshire
proverb, â€there is a bogy on every stile.â€ The cotch have even invented
a special bogyâ€”the amhanach or goblin which comes out at

The sungod himself is said to have instituted the ugust festival
called â€Lugnassadâ€ Lughâ€s commemoration in reland, â€Lla Lluanysâ€ in

the dn town of Lugus. The midsummer festival, on the other hand,

The characteristic features of these festivals give certain proof of
the original nature of the great pagan ceremonials of which they are
the survivals and travesties. n all of them, bonfires are
lighted on the highest hills, and the hearth fires solemnly rekindled.
They form the excuse for much sport and ollity. ut there is yet
something sinister in the air the â€fairiesâ€ are active and abroad,
and one must be careful to omit no prescribed rite, if one would avoid
kindling their anger or falling into their power. To some of these
stillhalfbelievedin naturegods offerings were made down to a
comparatively late period. hen ennant wrote, in the eighteenth
century, it was the custom on eltaineday in many ighland villages
to offer libations and cakes not only to the â€spiritsâ€ who were
believed to be beneficial to the flocks and herds, but also to
creatures like the fox, the eagle, and the hoodiecrow which so often
molested them. t alloweâ€en the eltic amhain the natives of
the ebrides used to pour libations of ale to a marine god called
hony, imploring him to send seaweed to the shore. n honour,
also, of such beings, curious rites were performed. Maidens washed
their faces in morning dew, with prayers for beauty. They carried
sprigs of the rowan, that mystic tree whose scarlet berries were the
ambrosial food of the Tuatha  anann.

n their original form, these now harmless rural holidays were
undoubtedly religious festivals of an orgiastic natureworship such as
became so popular in reece in connection with the cult of ionysus. The
great â€lords of lifeâ€ and of the powers of nature that made and ruled
life were propitiated by maddening invocations, by riotous dances, and
by human sacrifice.

The bonfires which fill so large a part in the modern festivals have
been casually mentioned. riginally they were no mere feux de oie,
but had a terrible meaning, which the customs connected with them
preserve. t the ighland eltaine, a cake was divided by lot, and
whoever drew the â€burnt pieceâ€ was obliged to leap three times over the
flames. t the midsummer bonfires in reland all passed through the
fire the men when the flames were highest, the women when they were
lower, and the cattle when there was nothing left but smoke. n ales,
upon the last day of ctober, the old amhain, there was a slightly
different, and still more suggestive rite. The hilltop bonfires were
watched until they were announced to be extinct. Then all would race
headlong down the hill, shouting a formula to the effect that the devil
would get the hindmost. The devil of a new belief is the god of the one
it has supplanted in all three instances, the custom was no mere
meaningless horseplay, but a symbolical human sacrifice.

 similar observance, but of a more cruel kind, was kept up in rance
upon t. ohnâ€s ay, until forbidden by law in the reign of Louis the
ourteenth. askets containing living wolves, foxes, and cats were
burned upon the bonfires, under the auspices and in the presence of the
sheriffs or the mayor of the town. aesar noted the custom among
the druids of constructing huge wickerwork images, which they filled
with living men, and set on fire, and it can hardly be doubted that the
wretched wolves, foxes, and cats were ceremonial substitutes for human
beings.

n ingenious theory was invented, after the introduction of
hristianity, with the purpose of allowing such ancient rites to
continue, with a changed meaning. The passing of persons and cattle
through flame or smoke was explained as a practice which interposed a
magic protection between them and the powers of evil. This homopathic
device of using the evil powerâ€s own sacred fire as a means of
protection against himself somewhat suggests that seething of the kid in
its motherâ€s milk which was reprobated by the Levitical law but, no
doubt, pagan â€demonsâ€ were considered fair game. The explanation, of
course, is an obviously and clumsily forced one it was the grim
druidical philosophy thatâ€”to uote aesarâ€”â€unless the life of man was
repaid for the life of man, the will of the immortal gods could not be
appeasedâ€ that dictated both the national and the private human
sacrifices of the elts, the shadows of which remain in the leaping
through the bonfires, and in the numerous recorded sacrifices of cattle
within uite recent times.

Mr. Laurence omme, in his thnology in olklore, has collected many
modern instances of the sacrifices of cattle not only in reland and
cotland, but also in ales, Yorkshire, orthamptonshire, ornwall, and
the sle of Man. â€ithin twenty miles of the metropolis of cotland
a relative of rofessor impson offered up a live cow as a sacrifice to
the spirit of the murrain.â€ n ales, when cattlesickness broke

rock. enerally, however, the wretched victims were burned alive. n

Tynwald ill, to avert the anger of the ghostly occupant of a barrow
which had been desecrated by opening. ometimes, even, these burnt
oblations were offered to an alleged hristian saint. The registers of
the resbytery of ingwall for the years  and  contain records

of a being whom some called â€t. Mourieâ€, and others, perhaps knowing
his doubtful character, â€ane god Mourieâ€. t irkcudbright, it was
t. uthbert, and at lynnog, in ales, it was t. euno, who was
thought to delight in the blood of bulls.

uch sacrifices of cattle appear mainly to have been offered to stay
plague among cattle. Man for man and beast for beast, was, perhaps, the
old rule. ut among all nations, human sacrifices have been gradually
commuted for those of animals. The family of the â€erlebys in

The third form of eltic pagan survival is found in numerous instances
of the adoration of water, trees, stones, and animals. Like the other
â€ryanâ€ nations, the elts worshipped their rivers. The ee received
divine honours as a wargoddess with the title of erfon, while the
ibble, under its name of elisama, was identified by the omans with
Minerva. Myths were told of them, as of the sacred streams of
reece. The ee gave oracles as to the results of the perpetual wars
between the elsh and the nglish as its stream encroached either upon
the elsh or the nglish side, so one nation or the other would be
victorious. The Tweed, like many of the reek rivers, was credited
with human descendants. That the rivers of reat ritain received
human sacrifices is clear from the folklore concerning many of them.
eprived of their expected offerings, they are believed to snatch by
stealth the human lives for which they crave. â€iver of art, iver of
art, every year thou claimest a heart,â€ runs the evonshire folksong.
The pey, too, reuires a life yearly, but the pirit of the ibble
is satisfied with one victim at the end of every seven years.

vidence, however, of the worship of rivers is scanty compared with that
of the adoration of wells. â€n the case of wellworship,â€ says Mr.

n some cases these wells were resorted to for the cure of diseases in
others, to obtain change of weather, or â€good luckâ€. fferings were made
to them, to propitiate their guardian gods or nymphs. ennant tells us
that in olden times the rich would sacrifice one of their horses at a
well near bergeleu, to secure a blessing upon the rest. owls were
offered at t. Teglaâ€s ell, near rexham, by epileptic patients.
ut of late years the wellspirits have had to be content with much
smaller tributesâ€”such trifles as pins, rags, coloured pebbles, and small
coins.

ith sacred wells were often connected sacred trees, to whose branches
rags and small pieces of garments were suspended by their humble
votaries. ometimes, where the ground near the well was bare of
vegetation, bushes were artificially placed beside the water. The same
people who venerated wells and trees would pay eual adoration to sacred
stones. Lord oden, describing, in , the sland of nniskea, off the
coast of Mayo, asserts that a sacred well called â€errivlaâ€ and a sacred
stone called â€eevougiâ€, which was kept carefully wrapped up in flannel
and brought out at certain periods to be publicly adored, seemed to be
the only deities known to that lone tlantic islandâ€s three hundred
inhabitants. t sounds incredible but there is ample evidence of
the worship of fetish stones by uite modern inhabitants of our islands.
The lan hattan kept such a stone in the sle of rran it was
believed, like the stone of nniskea, to be able to cure diseases, and
was kept carefully â€wrapped up in fair linen cloth, and about that there
was a piece of woollen clothâ€. imilarly, too, the worship of wells
was connected with the worship of animals. t a well in the â€evilâ€s
ausewayâ€, between uckley and cton, in hropshire, lived, and perhaps
still live, four frogs who were, and perhaps still are, believed to be
â€the devil and his impsâ€â€”that is to say, gods or demons of a proscribed
idolatry. n reland such guardian spirits are usually fishâ€”trout,
eels, or salmon thought to be endowed with eternal life. The genius
of a well in anffshire took the form of a fly, which was also said to
be undying, but to transmigrate from body to body. ts function was to
deliver oracles according as it seemed active or lethargic, its
votaries drew their omens. t is needless to multiply instances of
a still surviving cult of water, trees, stones, and animals. nough to
say that it would be easy. hat concerns us is that we are face to face
in ritain with living forms of the oldest, lowest, most primitive
religion in the worldâ€”one which would seem to have been once universal,
and which, crouching close to the earth, lets other creeds blow over it
without effacing it, and outlives one and all of them.

t underlies the three great worldreligions, and still forms the real
belief of perhaps the maority of their titular adherents. t is
characteristic of the wisdom of the hristian hurch that, knowing its
power, she sought rather to sanctify than to extirpate it. hat once
were the eltic euivalents of the reek â€fountains of the nymphsâ€ were
consecrated as â€holy wellsâ€. The process of so adopting them began
early. t. olumba, when he went in the sixth century to convert the
icts, found a spring which they worshipped as a god he blessed it, and
â€from that day the demon separated from the waterâ€. ndeed, he so
sanctified no less than three hundred such springs. acred stones
were eually taken under the gis of hristianity. ome were placed on
the altars of cathedrals, others built into consecrated walls. The
animal gods either found themselves the heroes of hristian legends, or
where, for some reason, such adoption was hopeless, were proclaimed
â€witchesâ€ animalsâ€, and dealt with accordingly. uch happened to the
hare, a creature sacred to the ancient ritons, but now in bad
odour among the superstitious. The wren, too, is hunted to death upon
t. tephenâ€s ay in reland. ts crime is said to be that it has â€a
drop of the deâ€ilâ€s blood in itâ€, but the real reason is probably to be
found in the fact that the rish druids used to draw auguries from its
chirpings.

                                              

e have made in this volume some attempt to draw a picture of the
ancient religion of our earliest ancestors, the aelic and the ritish
elts. e have shown what can be gathered of the broken remnants of a
mythology as splendid in conception and as brilliant in colour as that
of the reeks. e have tried to paint its divine figures, and to retell
their heroic stories. e have seen them fall from their shrines, and
yet, rising again, take on new lives as kings, or saints, or knights of
romance, and we have caught fading glimpses of them surviving today as
the â€fairiesâ€, their rites still cherished by worshippers who hardly
know who or why they worship. f necessity this survey has been brief
and incomplete. hether the great edifice of the eltic mythology will
ever be wholly restored one can at present only speculate. ts colossal
fragments are perhaps too deeply buried and too widely scattered. ut,
even as it stands ruined, it is a mighty uarry from which poets yet
unborn will hew spiritual marble for houses not made with hands.
